$a^{*}\mathrm{Mg}^*$ represents milligrams of theoretical fuel salt, that is, fuel salt containing the concentration of $^{89}\mathrm{Sr}$ it would contain after the actual power history if radioactive decay were the only loss mechanism. The fuel actually contained about $70\%$ of this concentration of $^{89}\mathrm{Sr}$ , since some $^{89}\mathrm{Kr}$ was lost to the off-gas.

submerged in the pump bowl salt, regardless of immersion time.

On the other hand, while the metal and graphite specimens are in the gas phase, before and after immersion, they are equally exposed to a rain of $^{89}\mathrm{Sr}$ from decaying krypton in the gas phase. If both surfaces are good sinks for elemental Sr, the equal deposition on both surfaces is explained. The exposures to the gas phase were for similar lengths of time for all these runs. The window capsule can protect the samples against a particulate dust or spray but not against truly gaseous species like $^{89}\mathrm{Kr}$ .

The deposition of the noble metal nuclides $\left({}^{99}\mathrm{Mo}, {}^{95}\mathrm{Nb}, {}^{111}\mathrm{Ag}, {}^{129}\mathrm{Te}, {}^{132}\mathrm{Te}, {}^{103}\mathrm{Ru}, \text{and } {}^{106}\mathrm{Ru}\right)$ and $^{131}\mathrm{I}$ on both graphite and Hastelloy N showed a definite but not very large variation with time of exposure. The 10- and 3-hr exposures showed about five times as much deposition of these nuclides as the 1-hr and 10-min exposures. However, the data from the 10- and 3-hr exposures were very similar, as was the case for the 1-hr and 10-min exposures. These data are best compared with each other and with the long-term surveillance data in terms of the rate equation:

$$
\frac {d N}{d t} = k - \lambda N,
$$

which for constant $k$ integrates to

$$
N = \frac {k}{\lambda} (1 - e ^ {- \lambda t}),
$$

where

$$
N = \text {a t o m s}
$$

$$
k = \text {d e p o s i t i o n r a t e}, \text {a t o m s} \mathrm {c m} ^ {- 2} \sec^ {- 1},
$$

$$
\lambda = \text {d e c a y} \quad \text {r a t e}, \sec^ {- 1}, \text {a n d}
$$

$$
t = \text {d e p o s i t i o n}
$$

Table 8.4 gives a comparison of deposition rates $(k$ 's) calculated from the 10-hr exposure data and from the fifth set of surveillance specimens. Reasons for the low 10-hr deposition rates of $^{99}\mathrm{Mo}$ , $^{132}\mathrm{Te}$ , and $^{131}\mathrm{I}$ on Hastelloy N are not known. However, a surprising number of the ratios of these rates are near 1, indicating nearly equal deposition rates in the pump bowl after 10 hr and in the core after about 2000 hr. Thus it appears that the reactor surfaces would not become saturated with noble metal fission products and would therefore continuously absorb them. It also appears that the original fast 10-min rates of deposition are reduced within about 10 hr to more moderate rates which then remain constant.

Table 8.4. Comparison of Long-Term and Short-Term Deposition Rates   

<table><tr><td rowspan="2">Isotope</td><td colspan="2">Ratio, 10-hr Rate/1786-hr Rate</td></tr><tr><td>Graphite</td><td>Hastelloy N</td></tr><tr><td>99Mo</td><td>1.18</td><td>0.138</td></tr><tr><td>95Nb</td><td>1.68</td><td>1.30</td></tr><tr><td>129Te</td><td>1.47</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>132Te</td><td></td><td>0.022(?)</td></tr><tr><td>103Ru</td><td>6.16</td><td>1.5</td></tr><tr><td>131I</td><td>24.8</td><td>0.31</td></tr></table>

The high 10-hr deposition rate of $^{131}\mathrm{I}$ on graphite resulting in the unusually high ratio of 24.8 in Table 8.4 is also difficult to explain. The deposition rate of $^{131}\mathrm{I}$ on graphite for the 3-hr exposure in the pump bowl was even higher. In both short-term tests the deposition of $^{131}\mathrm{I}$ was usually only slightly less than that of $^{132}\mathrm{Te}$ and usually higher than that of $^{129}\mathrm{Te}$ , measured in grams of theoretical salt per sample. In long-term core exposures, the deposition of tellurium usually exceeded that of $^{131}\mathrm{I}$ by an order of magnitude or more measured in the same units. This suggests that the 25-min $^{131}\mathrm{Te}$ originally deposited heavily, then decayed to $^{131}\mathrm{I}$ , which was slowly washed off by the fuel salt.

# 8.3 FISSION PRODUCT DEPOSITION ON THE FIFTH SET OF GRAPHITE AND HASTELLOY N SAMPLES FROM THE MSRE CORE

S. S. Kirslis F. F. Blankenship L. L. Fairchild

A fifth set of long-term surveillance specimens of graphite (Poco grade AXF-5Q) and Hastelloy N was exposed to fissioning molten fuel salt for 12,943 MWhr during the final runs 19 and 20 of the MSRE. These tubular specimens were especially designed to test the effects of surface roughness and different flow conditions on the deposition of fission products from the fuel salt on or into the solids. One of the specimen assemblies contained electron microscope screens for detecting particulate matter in the gas phase above stagnant molten fissioning fuel salt.

# 8.3.1 Effect of Surface Roughness

There was no variation of deposition of any fission product with surface roughness for either graphite or Hastelloy N. For graphite the adjacent samples were machined to surface roughnesses of 5, 25, and $125\mu \mathrm{in}$ rms. Most of the metal samples were machined to the same range of roughnesses, but they also included $1 / _{16}$ -in.-diam Hastelloy N wire samples with surfaces as drawn and one tube with surface as machined (of undetermined roughness).

Two different explanations are invoked for the lack of dependence of deposition on surface roughness, which clearly eliminates a simple adsorption theory. For the deposition of $^{95}\mathrm{Zr}$ , $^{141}\mathrm{Ce}$ , and $^{144}\mathrm{Ce}$ on graphite and of $^{89}\mathrm{Sr}$ , $^{95}\mathrm{Zr}$ , $^{137}\mathrm{Cs}$ , $^{140}\mathrm{Ba}$ , $^{141}\mathrm{Ce}$ , and $^{144}\mathrm{Ce}$ on Hastelloy N, the quantity deposited amounted only to the equivalent of contamination by 1 to 4 mg of fuel salt per square centimeter of surface. Fission recoil is thought to be the mechanism by which these species are injected into the graphite and metal surfaces. The much heavier (by two or three orders of magnitude) depositions of noble metal fission products ( $^{99}\mathrm{Mo}$ , $^{95}\mathrm{Nb}$ , $^{103}\mathrm{Ru}$ , $^{129}\mathrm{Te}$ , $^{132}\mathrm{Te}$ ) on metal and graphite are thought to be controlled by mass transfer through a stagnant film of salt adjacent to the surface. However, large differences in diffusion coefficients, particle size, or accommodation coefficients for the different elements must be invoked to make the simple mass transfer model fit the data.

A similar model for the mass transfer of the rare-gas precursors of $^{89}\mathrm{Sr}$ and $^{140}\mathrm{Ba}$ through a salt film is thought to account for the observed heavy deposition of these species in graphite. In the case of $^{137}\mathrm{Cs}$ a mobility of cesium in the graphite must be also postulated to account for the low concentration remaining in the graphite.

# 8.3.2 Effect of Flow Conditions on Deposition

The several tubular test specimens of graphite and Hastelloy N in the fifth set of surveillance specimens were intentionally arranged to provide some variety of flow conditions under which the fuel contacted the graphite and metal surfaces. The low pressure drops available made it impossible to extend the flow conditions into the turbulent range. The flow conditions varied only from stagnant fuel salt to linear velocities of about 1 fps, or Reynolds numbers of about 700. Turbulence promoter wires were wrapped around one graphite and one metal tube, although sizable effects on turbulence were not expected at these low velocities.

On the Hastelloy N specimens, deposition of fission products varied significantly from the average only for the tube containing stagnant fuel salt with a $1^{1/2}$ -in.-long gas-phase region above the salt. Deposition on Hastelloy N from stagnant salt was about the same as from flowing fuel for $^{89}\mathrm{Sr}$ , $^{95}\mathrm{Zr}$ , $^{137}\mathrm{Cs}$ , $^{140}\mathrm{Ba}$ , $^{141}\mathrm{Ce}$ , and $^{144}\mathrm{Ce}$ . As expected, heavy deposition of $^{89}\mathrm{Sr}$ and fivefold greater deposition of $^{140}\mathrm{Ba}$ than from salt took place from the gas phase on Hastelloy N. For $^{99}\mathrm{Mo}$ , deposition from stagnant fuel was even heavier than the heavy deposition from flowing fuel. There was equally heavy deposition of $^{99}\mathrm{Mo}$ on the metal surfaces contacting the gas phase. But the deposition from stagnant salt of $^{95}\mathrm{Nb}$ , $^{103}\mathrm{Ru}$ , $^{132}\mathrm{Te}$ , and $^{131}\mathrm{I}$ was lower by a factor of 10 than from flowing salt. The quantities of $^{95}\mathrm{Nb}$ , $^{103}\mathrm{Ru}$ , $^{106}\mathrm{Ru}$ , and $^{131}\mathrm{I}$ on gas-exposed Hastelloy N were another factor of 10 lower. The amounts of $^{132}\mathrm{Te}$ on Hastelloy N exposed to stagnant salt and to the gas phase were similar. The different behaviors of $^{99}\mathrm{Mo}$ , $^{132}\mathrm{Te}$ , and the remaining noble metal fission products are difficult to explain.

There was no graphite specimen exposed to stagnant fuel; thus no significant variations of deposition on the graphite specimens were observed.

It was rather disappointing that the above results did not confirm the film-controlled diffusion model more definitely. The decreased deposition of $^{95}\mathrm{Nb}$ , $^{103}\mathrm{Ru}$ , $^{106}\mathrm{Ru}$ , $^{132}\mathrm{Te}$ , and $^{131}\mathrm{I}$ from the stagnant fuel is in accord with the model. The behavior of $^{99}\mathrm{Mo}$ in the stagnant region is anomalous.

# 8.3.3 Comparison of Deposition of Noble Metal Fission Products on Graphite and Hastelloy N

One of the assemblies in the fifth set of surveillance specimens was designed expressly to compare fission product deposition on metal and graphite surfaces exposed to fissioning fuel under identical conditions. The fuel was made to flow in a $1/16$ -in.-wide annulus between a Hastelloy N core $1/2$ in. in diameter and a $5/8$ -in.-ID Poco graphite tube.

The deposition of noble metal fission products on these graphite and metal surfaces is compared in Table 8.5. The unit of activity used in the table is grams of theoretical salt per square centimeter of surface. The theoretical salt contains all of each nuclide which would be found in the fuel salt if decay were the only loss mechanism. This unit of comparison takes into account the power history of the fuel. Each value can be multiplied by the total area of graphite $(\sim 2\times 10^{6}\mathrm{cm}^{2})$ or metal $(\sim 1\times 10^{6}\mathrm{cm}^{2})$ and divided by the total grams of fuel $(\sim 5\times 10^{6})$ in the reactor to yield the

Table 8.5. Comparison of Fission Product Deposition on Graphite and Hastelloy N   

<table><tr><td></td><td>Adjacent Graphite</td><td>Adjacent Hastelloy N</td><td>Average Graphite</td><td>Average Hastelloy N</td></tr><tr><td>95Nb</td><td>0.36</td><td>0.45</td><td>0.30</td><td>0.54</td></tr><tr><td>99Mo</td><td>0.31</td><td>1.93</td><td>0.31</td><td>3.02</td></tr><tr><td>103Ru</td><td>0.12</td><td>0.18</td><td>0.09</td><td>0.23</td></tr><tr><td>106Ru</td><td>0.09</td><td>0.22</td><td>0.07</td><td>0.23</td></tr><tr><td>129Te</td><td>0.04</td><td></td><td>0.06</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>132Te</td><td></td><td>1.37</td><td></td><td>3.16</td></tr><tr><td>131I</td><td>0.007</td><td>0.24</td><td>0.005</td><td>0.30</td></tr></table>

fraction of total nuclide present to be found on the graphite or metal.

The last two columns of the table give the average depositions on the 12 graphite and the 13 metal surveillance specimens exposed to flowing fuel salt during the same period.

These results show that the deposition behavior on adjacent specimens is generally similar to that on geometrically separated samples and confirm the previously noted preference of most noble metal nuclides to deposit on metal rather than on graphite. This would argue for different accommodation coefficients for deposition on metal and graphite. In previous tests more $^{95}\mathrm{Nb}$ usually deposited on graphite than on metal.

# 8.3.4 Concentration Profiles of Fission Products in Graphite

The interior surfaces of the two graphite tubes from the fifth set of surveillance specimens (see ref. 9) were sampled in a hot cell with a special adjustable boring tool making successive cuts 1, 2, 3, 10, and 10 and 20 mils thick. The graphite powder samples were dissolved and analyzed radiochemically for ${}^{89}\mathrm{Sr}$ , ${}^{95}\mathrm{Zr}$ , ${}^{140}\mathrm{Ba}$ , ${}^{137}\mathrm{Cs}$ , ${}^{141}\mathrm{Ce}$ , ${}^{144}\mathrm{Ce}$ , ${}^{99}\mathrm{Mo}$ , ${}^{129}\mathrm{Te}$ , ${}^{132}\mathrm{Te}$ , ${}^{131}\mathrm{I}$ , ${}^{95}\mathrm{Nb}$ , ${}^{103}\mathrm{Ru}$ , and ${}^{106}\mathrm{Ru}$ . A sample of each graphite piece which remained after boring was analyzed to provide information on fission product deposition on the exterior surfaces of the graphite tubes.

The concentration profiles and total depositions per square centimeter obtained were generally similar to those from previous sets of surveillance specimens. As usual, except for $^{89}\mathrm{Sr}$ , $^{137}\mathrm{Cs}$ , and $^{140}\mathrm{Ba}$ the concentrations of fission products fell by two or more orders of magnitude in a few mils. For the three exceptions, each possessing a rare-gas precursor of appreciable half-life, the interior profiles were level, as previously observed for Poco graphite. The interior level averaged

$3.7 \times 10^{11}$ dis $\min^{-1} g^{-1}$ for $^{89} \mathrm{Sr}$ , about twice the average for Poco graphite in the fourth set of surveillance specimens and four times the average for Poco graphite in the third set. The interior concentrations for $^{140} \mathrm{Ba}$ (and most other isotopes) were similar in the three sets of specimens.

The $^{89}\mathrm{Sr}$ profiles were unusual also in that all five of them showed higher interior than surface concentrations. The first surface sample, about 1 mil thick, usually contained a concentration of about $2 \times 10^{11}$ dis min $^{-1}$ g $^{-1}$ of $^{89}\mathrm{Sr}$ , which rose to about $4 \times 10^{11}$ dis min $^{-1}$ g $^{-1}$ at a depth of 5 mils. Hints of similar behavior were noted for $^{140}\mathrm{Ba}$ and $^{137}\mathrm{Cs}$ . A reexamination of profiles for $^{89}\mathrm{Sr}$ , $^{140}\mathrm{Ba}$ , and $^{137}\mathrm{Cs}$ from previous surveillance specimens also frequently showed low concentrations in the first few mils from the salt-exposed surface. Typically, the concentrations would fall from a high initial value at the surface to a low value at a depth of 3 to 5 mils, then rise to a level intermediate value.

This mobility was anticipated for $^{137}\mathrm{Cs}$ , which is known to exhibit a volatility as the element or carbide. Some information on a high mobility of $^{89}\mathrm{Sr}$ and $^{140}\mathrm{Ba}$ diffusing through pyrolytic carbon coatings on $\mathrm{UO}_2$ and $\mathrm{UC}_2$ fuel particles is in accord with the observed profiles. Since the fuel salt or flush salt should be an effective sink for elemental $^{89}\mathrm{Sr}$ , $^{140}\mathrm{Ba}$ , and $^{137}\mathrm{Cs}$ , their diffusion from the interior of the graphite toward the salt could conceivably result in the observed concentration gradients, particularly during low- or zero-power operation.

# 8.3.5 Electron Microscope Examination of Particles in the Gas Phase Above Fuel Salt in the MSRE

The topmost test assembly in the fifth set of surveillance specimens was a Hastelloy N tube closed at the top end, open to flowing salt at the bottom end, and containing a central rod with 11 electron microscope screens attached every $\frac{1}{2}$ in. vertically. Post-irradiation examination of the rod and screens showed that during MSRE power operation the salt level in the tube rose so that only the top two screens were in the helium gas phase at the top of the tube. The long heating of the screens caused them to self-weld to the rod, so that it was not possible to remove the copper screens from the Hastelloy N rod without damaging the delicate carbon film on the screens.

With the electron microscope it was possible to see irregularly shaped particles with a sintered appearance on a few remaining scraps of carbon film. The particles were about 0.1 to $10\mu$ in size, occasionally strung

together in chains, and gave electron diffraction patterns suggestive of $\mathrm{Li}_2\mathrm{BeF}_4$ crystals. No other kinds of particles could be identified on the screens from the gas phase above the molten fissioning salt. Radiochemical analyses had shown that deposits on the gas-phase tube walls contained 6 to $9\times 10^{15}$ atoms of $^{99}\mathrm{Mo}$ per square centimeter (several monolayers) at reactor shutdown. Apparently this material did not collect into particles big enough to see with the electron microscope.

# 8.4 A POSSIBLE ORIGIN OF SMOKES AND MISTS EMITTED BY MSRE FUEL

# F. F. Blankenship

The fact that fluoride fuels are strongly nonwetting toward clean metals is well established. The consequences of such interfacial behavior for very small metallic particles or even single atoms are perhaps novel, if not surprising.

In the first place, a particle which is at the melt surface is on the surface rather than in the surface as in more familiar cases. This is because the interfacial energies are less the smaller the area of liquid-solid contact. A particle at the surface is expelled from the surface, and work would be required to cause the particle to penetrate the surface again.

As diagrammed simply in Fig. 8.4, once out of the liquid the particle tends to remain out. Figure 8.4 shows the interfacial energy levels, $E$ , over a distance interval $(d)$ that includes a liquid-gas interface.

![](images/31d05dc1f6969311534c419d4889c4af885885fefa62f4ad348fb5a1d3e072e1.jpg)  
Fig. 8.4. Surface Energy Levels for a Nonwetted Particle on Either Side of a Liquid-Gas Interface.

The question arises whether, as a particle moves from a higher potential energy in the liquid to a lower energy state outside, part of the potential energy difference is converted to kinetic energy.

Looking at the reverse process we can calculate the minimum kinetic energy a metal particle must possess to penetrate the surface as it collides with the liquid interface. A new liquid-metal interface having the area of the particle is created. The assumption is made that the increase in interfacial energy is at least as great as for this much newly created liquid area. The surface energy of the liquid is numerically equal to the surface tension, and in the case of MSRE fuel is about 200 ergs/cm². The surface tension increases as temperature decreases.

Considering a single atom of molybdenum (diameter 2.80 Å), the surface area is $2.46 \times 10^{-15} \, \text{cm}^2$ and the energy increment is $4.92 \times 10^{-13} \, \text{erg}$ . Thus the minimum velocity required is $8 \times 10^4 \, \text{cm/sec}$ . For comparison, the most probable velocity for gas molecules is $\sqrt{2RT / M}$ which for molybdenum at $900^\circ \text{K}$ is $4 \times 10^4 \, \text{cm/sec}$ .

The notion of using for the surface energy of a single atom the same value as for an ordinary interface seems somewhat unrealistic. However, this procedure gives the correct answers in calculating the solubility of rare gases in liquids and in nucleation theory.

If, when the liquid surface collapses as the particle is ejected, droplets are also jetted, as when a gas bubble collapses, we have an explanation of both the smoke and the mist that seem to arise from the fuel.

Another peculiar consequence of the nonwetting interface is that a single atom is already a critical nucleus. In other words, there is no surface energy barrier to the growth of any size clump. Thus the only hindrance to homogeneous or bulk nucleation of metals in the melt is the great dilution. We suspect that not much nucleation occurs there or else the metals would form alloys and travel together. Usually for homogeneous nucleation about 200 atoms are required to form a critical nucleus.

According to the hypothesis presented here, the differences noted in the behavior of various metal fission product atoms are to be attributed to differences in wettability which alter the value of 200 ergs/cm² used in Fig. 8.4.

This proposed mechanism is compatible with the observed behavior of smokes arising from quiescent fuels where there are no bubbles. It explains why the phenomena can be demonstrated at the tracer level with $^{95}\mathrm{Zr} - ^{95}\mathrm{Nb}$ , again without bubbles. It also explains why we were unable to prepare stable metallic colloids in the

fuel. It allows the diffusion in the liquid to be the rate-controlling step and does not depend on the nature of the surface in contact with the liquid.

At first glance the particle rejection by the fuel, as proposed here, is at variance with the fact that during the later operation of the MSRE the noble metals were sometimes found in the fuel and sometimes not, in an erratic fashion. The explanation may lie in the fact that fuel analyses detected sludges that were rafted around and were sometimes sampled and sometimes not. The sludge was probably not in the liquid in the sense that a wetted or soluble particle might be. Also, the sludge was alloyed, in that the proportions of the metals were not erratic.

Another prediction that can be made with confidence is that a nonwetted particle on the surface of a liquid could, if small enough, be dislodged by the molecular movements causing Brownian motion.

# 8.5 SYNTHESIS OF NIOBIUM FLUORIDES

C.F.Weaver J.S.Gill

Small (1-g) samples of niobium tetrafluoride were synthesized by the reduction of excess $\mathrm{NbF}_5$ with niobium metal in quartz containers at $200^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . The product was identified by x-ray diffraction analysis. It is currently being used in studies of the disproportionation reactions of the lower fluorides of niobium. A portion of the tetrafluoride was supplied to J. B. Bates and G. E. Boyd for use in Raman spectra studies.

Prolonged heating of $\mathrm{NbF_4}$ at $250^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ under vacuum in a quartz tube produced $\mathrm{NbF_5}$ and a material different than Nb, $\mathrm{NbF_4}$ , and $\mathrm{NbF_5}$ which by mass spectrometric analysis contained few impurities, suggesting that this was a niobium fluoride with valence less than 4.

These studies will be continued in order to determine the conditions under which the niobium fluorides are stable and under which the disproportionations may be used to synthesize the lower fluorides.

# 8.6 MOLYBDENUM AND NIOBIUM FLUORIDE SOLUTIONS IN MOLTEN $\mathbf{L}\mathbf{i}_2\mathbf{B}\mathbf{e}\mathbf{F}_4$

C. F. Weaver H. A. Friedman

J. S. Gill

The addition of graphite (6 g of graphite per kilogram of melt) to molten $\mathrm{Li}_2\mathrm{BeF}_4$ at $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ containing 530

![](images/8542bd5ae0be688833fb5afc17715eceb2adf273ac48954b6418121ebebe4cdc.jpg)  
Fig. 8.5. Removal of $\mathbf{Mo}^{3+}$ from Molten $\mathbf{Li}_2\mathbf{BeF}_4$ .

ppm of $\mathbf{Mo}^{3+}$ had no significant effect on the rate of loss of molybdenum from the system. The experiment was repeated, and the results are presented in Figs. 8.5 and 8.6. All of the kinetic experiments with this system $^{12-18}$ at $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ indicate that the molybdenum left the system by a half-order process with a rate constant, defined as

$$
K = t ^ {- 1} \left(C _ {0} ^ {1 / 2} - C ^ {1 / 2}\right),
$$

![](images/85c2f3e6a49a1a6dc7021a9c6f123e37b33221f2a19b374b5814c91e4d31db63.jpg)  
Fig. 8.6. Removal of $\mathbf{Mo}^{3+}$ from Molten $\mathbf{Li}_2\mathbf{BeF}_4$ .

of $7 \times 10^{-3} \mathrm{ppm}^{-1 / 2} \mathrm{hr}^{-1}$ . This behavior was not affected by the helium flow rate, surface area of the copper container, presence of $\mathrm{UF_4}$ , presence of graphite, or the quantity of molybdenum metal produced. In no case has the copper container been oxidized by the molybdenum solutions. These observations lead to the conclusion that trivalent molybdenum in molten $\mathrm{Li_2BeF_4}$ at $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ was removed from the system by a homogeneous disproportionation reaction.

The behavior of similar solutions at higher temperatures is not as well understood at present. The few experiments which we have conducted at $700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ suggest $^{12-15}$ that the loss of molybdenum occurred more rapidly than at $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , the order of removal was variable and higher than at $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , and the removal rate was flow dependent. A recent experiment was started at $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ and finished at $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ as shown in Fig. 8.7. It displayed a much more rapid loss of molybdenum than a similar experiment which differed only in that the surface area of the copper container was less by an order of magnitude. These experiments suggest that the mechanism of removal of $\mathrm{Mo}^{3+}$ from molten $\mathrm{Li}_2\mathrm{BeF}_4$ is different in the 600 to $700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ temperature range than near $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . Such observations are consistent with the previously reported mass spectrometric results $^{15,16,19}$ that near $500^{\circ}$ only $\mathrm{MoF}_6$ and $\mathrm{MoF}_5$ are found in the vapor above disproportionating $\mathrm{MoF}_3$ . Above $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ $\mathrm{MoF}_4$ occurs and becomes an increasingly important species as the temperature rises.

![](images/759852ccecd2a78736c8d75490abb5e63acba82dc72a61cf1263adb2d9727d42.jpg)  
Fig. 8.7. Removal of $\mathrm{Mo}^{3+}$ from Molten $\mathrm{Li}_2\mathrm{BeF}_4$

Evidence that niobium metal was oxidized in the MSRE when the $\mathrm{U}^{3+}/\mathrm{U}^{4+}$ ratios were low led to the suggestion that its behavior might provide a means of monitoring the redox potential of such a system. In this connection the reactions

$$
\mathrm {N b} + x \mathrm {H F} (\mathrm {g}) \rightleftharpoons (x / 2) \mathrm {H} _ {2} (\mathrm {g}) + \mathrm {N b F} _ {x} (\mathrm {d})
$$

and

$$
\mathrm {N b F} _ {x} (\mathrm {d}) + \mathrm {L i} \rightarrow \mathrm {N b} + x \mathrm {L i F}
$$

in molten $\mathbf{L}\mathbf{i}_2\mathbf{B}\mathbf{e}\mathbf{F}_4$ are being investigated.17,18

The solutions produced by hydrofluorination of niobium metal at $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ in molten $\mathrm{Li}_2\mathrm{BeF}_4$ have been found to be stable for periods as long as a month with no evidence of a drop in concentration and have shown strong resistance to reduction with $\mathbf{H}_2$ gas. Consequently a stronger reductant, lithium metal, was used to reduce the $\mathrm{Nb}^{x+}$ and to determine its valence. Over the concentration range 340 to $2990~\mathrm{ppm}$ $\mathrm{Nb}^{x+}$ the valence was determined to be $3.8 \pm 0.3$ . We detected no obvious trend in the value with concentration. The fractional value may be caused by a mixture of species, a cluster compound, or simply experimental error in the determination. The value of $P_{\mathrm{HF}} / P_{\mathrm{H}}^{1/2}$ was near $10^{-4}$ atm $^{1/2}$ and varied by about $20\%$ over the concentration range 1000 to 1600 ppm of $\mathrm{Nb}^{x+}$ consistent with the high valence mentioned

above. This behavior with respect to reduction by hydrogen indicates that niobium is only slightly more noble than chromium and considerably more reactive than iron. These observations should not be extrapolated to other temperatures, since there is evidence in the literature $^{20}$ that both the valence and the stability of the niobium fluorides are strongly temperature dependent.

# 8.7 MASS SPECTROSCOPY OF NIOBIUM FLUORIDES

C.F.Weaver J.D.Redman

Earlier work $^{16,17,21}$ on the mass spectroscopy of niobium fluorides consisted in observing the pentafluoride, the associated oxyfluoride impurities, and the fluorination of niobium metal. More recently the disproportionation of the lower fluorides was given attention. We have previously reported that both a dimer and trimer of $\mathrm{NbF_5}$ have been observed. $^{21}$ The ionization efficiency curves and appearance potentials for the fragments of these species were given. $^{16,17,21}$ The cracking pattern for $\mathrm{NbOF_3}$ was tabulated as well as that for the complex polymer mixture of pentavalent niobium fluorides. We have recently attempted to resolve this composite pattern into the cracking patterns for the individual pentavalent species. Tentative cracking patterns based on the assumption that the vapor over $\mathrm{NbF_5}$ crystals at $75^{\circ}C$ is essentially all $\mathrm{Nb}_2\mathrm{F}_{10}$ are:

<table><tr><td rowspan="2">Ion</td><td colspan="2">Relative Intensity</td></tr><tr><td>Dimer</td><td>Monomer</td></tr><tr><td>NbF4+</td><td>100</td><td>100</td></tr><tr><td>NbF3+</td><td>16</td><td>10</td></tr><tr><td>NbF2+</td><td>4</td><td>13</td></tr><tr><td>NbF+</td><td>1</td><td>7</td></tr><tr><td>Nb+</td><td>0.3</td><td>3</td></tr><tr><td>Nb2F9+</td><td>45</td><td></td></tr></table>

These studies have also shown that at the higher temperatures both fluorination of niobium metal as well as the disproportion of $\mathrm{NbF_4}$ produce, in addition to $\mathrm{NbF}_5$ , at least one lower fluoride of niobium, probably $\mathrm{NbF}_3$ , in the vapor phase.

Analysis of the $\mathrm{NbF_4}$ disproportionation experiments, which are still in progress, suggests that $\mathrm{NbF_4}$ disproportionates above $100^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . This is consistent with the disproportionation of $\mathrm{NbF_4}$ in quartz at $250^{\circ}$ noted above (Sect. 10.3). That these temperatures are somewhat lower than those in the literature $^{20}$ is most likely a reflection of low operating pressure and high sensitivity of the mass spectrometer. In the temperature range $100 - 300^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ the vapor over disproportionating $\mathrm{NbF_4}$ was nearly all $\mathrm{NbF_5}$ . Above $300^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ the vapor contained, in addition to $\mathrm{NbF_5}$ , a lower niobium fluoride, probably $\mathrm{NbF_3}$ . A typical vapor composition in the temperature range 320 to $550^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ was $89\%$ $\mathrm{NbF_5}$ , $1\%$ $\mathrm{Nb_2F_{10}}$ , and $10\%$ $\mathrm{NbF_3}$ . The low-temperature instability of pure $\mathrm{NbF_4}$ contrasts sharply with the stability of the $\mathrm{NbF_{3,8}}$ solutions in molten $\mathrm{Li_2BeF_4}$ at $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ described in Sect. 10.4. The temperature dependence of the equilibrium shown in Fig. 8.8 yields a $\Delta H^{\circ}$ of 7 kcal per mole of $\mathrm{Nb_2F_{10}}$ in the same temperature range.

Use of the cracking patterns tabulated above to analyze the results of niobium fluorination indicates that the reaction $\mathrm{Nb} + \mathrm{F}_2$ yields essentially pure $\mathrm{NbF}_5$ with a few percent $\mathrm{Nb}_2\mathrm{F}_{10}$ between 150 and $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . At temperatures between 650 and $900^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ the trifluoride also appeared to be present. A typical yield was $83\%$ $\mathrm{NbF}_5$ , $11\%$ $\mathrm{Nb}_2\mathrm{F}_{10}$ , and $6\%$ $\mathrm{NbF}_3$ .

![](images/a0367c8ef7ced3bf9e5e499e5335bc89b74b345b25a63163dfe53b10fbe00403.jpg)  
Fig. 8.8. Enthalpy of the Reaction $\mathbf{Nb}_2\mathbf{F}_{10} \rightleftharpoons 2\mathbf{Nb}\mathbf{F}_5$ .

# 8.8 RAMAN SPECTRA OF CRYSTALLINE $\mathbf{MoF}_5$ AND $\mathbf{MoF}_4$

John B. Bates George E. Boyd

The infrared and Raman spectra of liquid and polycrystalline $\mathrm{MoF}_5$ have been reported by Quellette et al. The vibrational spectrum of molten $\mathrm{MoF}_5$ was interpreted on the basis of $D_{3h}$ molecular symmetry (trigonal bipyramid), but no attempt was made to assign the infrared or Raman spectrum of the polycrystalline material. The Raman spectrum of crystalline $\mathrm{MoF}_4$ has not, to the best of our knowledge, been previously reported.

We have remeasured the room-temperature Raman spectrum of crystalline $\mathrm{MoF}_5$ under conditions of higher resolution than were employed previously. The spectrum, shown in Fig. 8.9, was recorded with a Jarrell-Ash model 25-300 spectrophotometer using the 4880-Å line of a Spectra Physics 141 argon ion laser as the exciting source. The spectral slit width (resolution) was set at between 1 and $2\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ on different scans. Samples of $\mathrm{MoF}_5$ which had been sublimed onto the upper walls of a quartz reaction flask were employed. Spectra of $\mathrm{MoF}_4$ (Fig. 8.10) were obtained from a yellowish-tan solid contained in the bottom of the reaction flask using the 6328-Å line of a helium-neon laser. Both $\mathrm{MoF}_5$ and $\mathrm{MoF}_4$ were obtained in one stage of a process leading to the synthesis of $\mathrm{MoF}_3$ . By

22T. J. Quellette, C. T. Ratcliffe, and D. W. A. Sharp, J. Chem. Soc. (A) 1969, 2351.   
$^{23}$ C. F. Weaver and H. A. Friedman, MSR Program Semiann Progr. Rept. Aug. 31, 1969, ORNL-4449, pp. 114-15.

![](images/e764aa517d82b4253a9a6feb938f7f891fcfd2515680516ea5de12ef7d670e83.jpg)  
Fig. 8.9. Raman Spectrum of Crystalline $\mathbf{MoF}_5$ Measured at $298^{\circ}\mathbf{K}$ .

![](images/9496ee60f1906a52b5089479bec8522ec2794ae7c0daa777d36564acbed9c5ca.jpg)  
Fig. 8.10. Raman Spectrum of Crystalline $\mathrm{MoF_4}$ Measured at $298^{\circ}\mathrm{K}$ .

exciting the Raman spectra of these materials when they were contained in the evacuated reaction flask, it was not necessary to interrupt the synthesis of $\mathrm{MoF}_3$ at this stage.

Raman spectra of crystalline $\mathbf{NbF}_5$ and $\mathrm{TaF}_5$ , which have the same crystal structure as $\mathbf{MoF}_5$ ,[24] were recently reported by Beattie and coworkers.[25] The observed bands were assigned from a normal coordinate analysis of the $\mathbf{Nb}_4\mathbf{F}_{20}$ and $\mathbf{Ta}_4\mathbf{F}_{20}$ tetrameric structures which are contained in the primitive unit cell of the respective crystal lattices.[26,27] Of particular importance to the subject of this report, it was assumed in ref. 25 that coupling between the four corners of the tetramers across the fluoride bridges was small.

From the structure determination of Edwards et al., $^{24}$ MoF $_5$ crystallizes in the monoclinic system, and the lattice belongs to the $C_{2h}^3$ ( $C_2 / m$ ) space group. There are 24 atoms in the primitive cell in which the four molybdenum atoms are joined through fluoride bridges to form a square tetrameric unit, as shown in Fig. 8.11. Two of the molybdenum atoms located at opposite corners of the tetramer occupy $C_2$ sites, while the remaining (opposite) pair of molybdenum atoms

occupy $C_s$ sites. Using the structural data of ref. 24 and standard procedures, the irreducible representation for the 69 optical ( $k = 0$ ) modes of crystalline $\mathrm{MoF}_5$ is given by

$$
\Gamma^ {\mathrm {o p}} \left(C _ {2 h}\right) = 1 9 A _ {g} + 1 7 B _ {g} + 1 5 A _ {u} + 1 8 B _ {u}.
$$

Thus, based on $C_{2h}$ selection rules, $36k = 0$ modes of crystalline $\mathrm{MoF}_5$ are Raman active and the remaining 33 optical modes are infrared active. The observed spectrum of crystalline $\mathrm{MoF}_5$ (Fig. 8.9 and ref. 22), however, appears to be much less complex than expected from the above selection rules. A comparison of the Raman spectrum of crystalline $\mathrm{MoF}_5$ (Fig. 8.9)

![](images/0cf821e3a9be2c5ca48fd0189311add08d781cd114277e73ef8d8de003562f8e.jpg)  
Fig. 8.11. The Square Tetrameric Unit of the Molybdenum Pentafluoride Structure. $^{24}$

with that of the molten material suggested to us an interpretation of the crystal spectrum based on a double-correlation scheme.

The correlation diagrams which map the symmetry species of the $D_{3h}$ (molecular) group of $\mathrm{MoF}_5$ onto the species of the $C_{2h}$ factor group through the $C_2$ and $C_s$ site groups are presented in Table 8.6. The $\sigma_h$ orientation of the $C_s$ group with respect to the $D_{3h}$ group was chosen (Table 8.6) after consideration of the arrangement of fluorine atoms about the " $C_s$ " corners of the $\mathrm{Mo}_4\mathrm{F}_{20}$ tetramer. The correct orientation is not clear in this case, but this is a minor point in the following discussion and in view of the presently available spectral data. The 12 MoF $s$ fundamental modes assuming $D_{3h}$ symmetry are described by $2A_{1}^{\prime} + 2A_{2}^{\prime \prime} + 3E^{\prime} + E^{\prime \prime}$ . In the limit of zero (or very weak) coupling between adjacent corners of the $\mathrm{Mo}_4\mathrm{F}_{20}$ tetramer, the origin of the MoF $s$ $k = 0$ crystal vibrations can be determined from Table 10.6. For example, the $\nu_{1}(A_{1}^{\prime})$ molecular mode gives rise to two $A_g$ components in the crystal, $A_{g}^{a}$ and $A_{g}^{b}$ , where $a$ and $b$ denote that the factor group states arise, respectively, from a $C_s$ and a $C_2$ site state. The observed splitting between the Raman-active $(A_g)$ components of $\nu_{1}$ is caused by the difference in the static fields at the two (nonequivalent) sites occupied by molybdenum atoms $(C_s$ and $C_2)$ .

The remaining assignments of the Raman spectrum of crystalline $\mathrm{MoF}_5$ proposed in Table 8.7 follow from the example of $\nu_{1}$ discussed above: The $A_2^{\prime \prime}$ modes ( $\nu_{3}$ and $\nu_{4}$ ) give rise to two $B_g$ crystal components (Table 8.6), but only single, weak bands were assigned to these modes (Table 8.7). Four Raman-active crystal components are derived from the $E^{\prime}$ modes ( $A_g$ and $B_g$ from $C_2$ site states and two $A_g$ from $C_s$ site states). However, only two components each were assigned to $\nu_{6}$ and $\nu_{7}$ , and a single component of $\nu_{5}$ was identified at 747 cm $^{-1}$ (Fig. 8.9 and Table 8.7). Additional components

$b_{\mathbf{s}} =$ strong, $\mathfrak{m} =$ medium, $\mathbf{w} =$ weak, $\mathbf{vw} =$ very weak.

of $\nu_{s}$ are probably obscured by the strong $\nu_{1}(A_{1}^{\prime})$ components. The $E''$ modes, which give rise to four Raman-active components ( $A_{g}$ and $B_{g}$ from $C_{2}$ site states and two $B_{g}$ from $C_{s}$ site states), exhibited only single, weak components in the Raman spectrum of crystalline $\mathrm{MoF}_5$ (Table 8.7).

The assignment of the Raman spectrum of crystalline $\mathrm{MoF}_5$ proposed in Table 8.7 successfully accounts for both the number and intensity of the observed crystal components, especially in the stretching mode region, in which two strong components are observed for each of the $A_1'$ modes. The treatment of Beattie et al.25 could probably be applied in assigning the modes of crystalline $\mathrm{MoF}_5$ , but the analysis should be based on a tetrameric structure having $C_{2h}$ symmetry rather than $D_{2h}$ symmetry assumed in ref. 24.

The crystal structure of $\mathbf{MoF}_4$ has not been determined, so that a satisfactory interpretation of the Raman spectrum (Fig. 8.10) cannot be given at this time. However, we can tentatively identify the crystal components as originating from $\nu_{1}(A_{1}),\nu_{2}(E),\nu_{3}$ $(F_{2})$ , and $\pmb{\nu}_{4}$ $(F_{2})$ modes of an assumed tetrahedral MoF4.

The strong band at $722\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ is a totally symmetric component of $\nu_{1}(A_{1})$ , and one of the weaker bands at 746, 710, and $690~\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ may be a second component of $\nu_{1}$ (the $746\text{-cm}^{-1}$ band is a likely candidate). Two of the three weaker bands observed in this region are probably crystal components of the $\nu_{3}(F_{2})$ asymmetric

stretching mode. Alternatively, all three of the weak bands (746, 710, and $690\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ ) may be correlation field components of $\nu_{3}(F_{2})$

The degenerate bending modes of $\mathrm{MoF_4}$ are probably giving rise to the low-frequency components observed at 280, 251, and $211~\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ and possibly to the low-frequency shoulder on the $251\text{-cm}^{-1}$ band observed at about $220~\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ (Fig. 8.10). The bands observed at 176 and $142~\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ may be due to external modes of the $\mathrm{MoF_4}$ lattice, as these frequencies have about the correct magnitudes expected for librational motions of the $\mathrm{MoF_4}$ tetrahedra. However, the $176\text{-cm}^{-1}$ band could also be a correlation field component of one of the degenerate bending modes $(\nu_{2}$ or $\nu_{4})$

The crystal structure of $\mathbf{MoF}_5$ is isomorphic to that of $\mathrm{NbF_4}$ ; hence, we might suppose that $\mathbf{MoF_4}$ has the

same structure as $\mathrm{NbF_4}$ as determined by Gortsema and Didchenko.28 A group theoretical analysis of the $\mathrm{NbF_4}$ structure $(D_{4h}^{17})$ , however, shows that only two $k = 0$ modes are Raman active:

$$
\Gamma^ {\mathrm {o p}} = A _ {1 g} + E _ {g} + 2 A _ {2 u} + B _ {2 u} + 3 E _ {u}.
$$

In view of the spectrum shown in Fig. 8.10, it would not appear that the sample of $\mathrm{MoF_4}$ observed in this work has the $\mathrm{NbF_4}$ structure.

# 9. Properties of the Alkali Fluoroborates

# 9.1 SOLUBILITY OF $\mathbf{BF}_3$ GAS IN FLUORIDE MELTS

S. Cantor W. T. Ward

The purposes of this program are: (1) to use $\mathrm{BF}_3$ solubility as a thermodynamic probe to determine changes in fluoride ion activity and (2) to provide data on how $\mathrm{BF}_3$ might be used as a burnable neutron poison for purposes of reactor control.

The initial data in this program were obtained on $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2}$ (66-34 mole %) employing a double-pot apparatus similar to that described by Shaffer et al.,1,2 in which the salt was saturated with $\mathrm{BF}_3$ in one vessel, after which a portion was transferred through a connecting tube to a second vessel where the transferred melt was stripped of $\mathrm{BF}_3$ by sparging with helium. For greater experimental convenience we have employed a single-pot apparatus in which the vessel of $900~\mathrm{cm}^3$ volume was fabricated from $2^{1 / 2}$ -in. sched 40 nickel pipe.

The experimental procedure consists, as before, of saturating the molten salt at constant pressure and temperature and stripping with helium. After exiting from the vessel the helium- $\mathrm{BF}_3$ stream passes through columns of aqueous NaF solution which remove the $\mathrm{BF}_3$ . The absorbed $\mathrm{BF}_3$ is analyzed by mannitol titration. The vapor space above the melt at the end of the saturation consists solely of $\mathrm{BF}_3$ gas; this quantity of gas, easily calculated for the temperature-pressure conditions of the run, is subtracted from the total $\mathrm{BF}_3$ analyzed in the absorber.

The saturating time has been about 4 hr for virtually all the runs. Two runs in which 1-hr saturation times were used yielded $\mathrm{BF}_3$ solubilities 9 to $10\%$ lower than the 4-hr runs for the same temperatures and pressures. Stripping data indicate that with a helium flow rate of

100 cm³/min, 4 hr is sufficient to remove >99% of the BF₃ from the LiF-BeF₂ melt.

The main advantage of the single-pot apparatus is the elimination of the melt-transferring procedures. In the double-pot apparatus, the two transfers per run required at least 1 hr, sometimes longer because dip lines frequently plugged during the transfer operations. There appear to be no disadvantages in the single-pot system as long as the solubility of $\mathbf{BF}_3$ is great enough so that $\mathbf{BF}_3$ in the gas space above the melt is small compared with that dissolved in the melt. In LiF-BeF $_2$ (66-34 mole %), the ratio of $\mathbf{BF}_3$ in the gas space to that in the liquid varied from 0.032 at $523^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ to 0.18 at $723^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ (the melt occupying $\sim 60\%$ of the volume of the vessel).

In the pressure range studied, 1 to 2.5 atm, Henry's law was obeyed. A plot of Henry's law constants obtained by the single-pot procedure is given by the solid line in Fig. 9.1 using

$$
\ln K = - 2 2. 1 / R + 1 5, 9 0 0 / (R T),
$$

where $R$ is the gas constant, 1.987, and $T$ is in degrees Kelvin. The line and the scatter for the data obtained in the double-pot procedure are also shown for comparison, as are the data reported by Shaffer et al. for $\mathrm{BF}_3$ solubility in $\mathrm{LiF - BeF}_2 - \mathrm{ZrF}_4 - \mathrm{ThF}_4 - \mathrm{UF}_4$ (65-28-5-1-1 mole%). In the latter melt the solubility of $\mathrm{BF}_3$ is about two-thirds that in $\mathrm{LiF - BeF}_2$ (66-34 mole%). The lesser $\mathrm{BF}_3$ solubility is consistent with the hypothesis that, in fluoride melts containing alkali fluoride as the major component, $\mathbf{M}^{4+}$ ion "combines" with more fluoride ions (and therefore makes fluoride ions less available for interaction with $\mathrm{BF}_3$ ) than does $\mathrm{Be}^{2+}$ ion.

Solubilities of gaseous HF have also been measured² in LiF-BeF₂ (66-34 mole %), and inert gases have been measured³ in a slightly different melt (64-36 mole %).

![](images/0150052a6e3be6e37e20c1f376d236b132bae71aff0f832ac9466189044bcf62.jpg)  
Fig. 9.1. Solubility of $\mathbf{BF}_3$ in LiF-BeF $_2$ (66-34 Mole%).

Table 9.1. Gas Solubility in LiF-BeF ${}_{2}$   

<table><tr><td rowspan="2"></td><td colspan="3">Henry&#x27;s Law Constant (moles of gas per liter of melt per atmosphere)</td><td rowspan="2">ΔH° of Solubility</td></tr><tr><td>500°C</td><td>600°C</td><td>700°C</td></tr><tr><td>BF3a</td><td>4.6 × 10-1</td><td>1.4 × 10-1</td><td>0.56 × 10-1</td><td>-15.9</td></tr><tr><td>HFb</td><td>1.9 × 10-1</td><td>1.3 × 10-1</td><td>0.89 × 10-1</td><td>-5.98</td></tr><tr><td>HeC</td><td>7.5 × 10-5</td><td>1.2 × 10-4</td><td>1.5 × 10-4</td><td>5.2</td></tr><tr><td>XeC</td><td>1.0 × 10-6</td><td>2.3 × 10-6</td><td>5.1 × 10-6</td><td>12.1</td></tr></table>

${}^{a}$ Melt composition,66-34 mole %.   
$b$ Also in 66-34 mole $\%$ ref.2.   
cMelt composition, 64-36 mole $\%$ ref. 3.

These data are summarized and compared with $\mathbf{BF}_3$ solubility in Table 9.1. Note that $\mathbf{BF}_3$ and HF solubilities decrease with increasing temperature. For $\mathbf{BF}_3$ the magnitude of the enthalpy $(\Delta H^{\circ})$ of solubility

suggests that a strong chemical interaction occurs in the melt (perhaps the reaction $\mathrm{BF}_3(\mathbf{g}) + \mathbf{F}^{-}\rightleftharpoons \mathbf{BF}_4$ ).

There is a qualitative difference in the solubility of $\mathrm{BF}_3$ and HF, on one hand, as compared with the solubility of the inert gases. Note in Table 9.1 that the inert gas solubilities are about four orders of magnitude less and that solubility increases with temperature. The interaction of inert gas and the melt is probably "physical" (in contrast to "chemical") in which dissolution has been effectively represented $^4$ as the inert gas making bubbles of atomic dimensions by working against the surface tension of the liquid.

# 9.2 STUDIES OF HYDROGEN EVOLUTION AND TRITIUM EXCHANGE IN FLUOROBORATE COOLANT SALT

S. Cantor R. M. Waller

Investigations that seek to determine the extent to which chemically bound hydrogen in low concentrations will be retained in fluoroborate salts in the presence of metals of construction were continued. If it were noncorrosive, bound hydrogen in low concentrations in the melt would afford a reservoir for isotopic exchange with tritium diffusing into the MSBR coolant circuit. The metals of construction are those (Ni, Mo, Cr, Fe) which constitute the heat exchanger, steam generator-superheater, and the coolant drain tanks.

The main feature of the experimental apparatus used in this investigation is a silica vessel connected to a manifold from which gas samples can be removed or added (e.g., tritium gas can be introduced). Evacuated capsules containing salt and metal coupons were positioned within the silica vessel. The silica vessel fits into a tube furnace. The apparatus was described previously.[5]

The conditions and observations of the experimental runs are given in Table 9.2. In all cases the salt used, $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ NaF (92-8 mole %), has an analyzed $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$ content of $120\pm 60$ ppm. In the analysis the moisture was extracted from the salt with pyridine, and the pyridine-water azeotrope was subsequently distilled into Karl Fischer reagent. All capsules were constructed of nickel.

The most significant experiment is run 5. The tritium analysis of the salt shows that very little tritium

Table 9.2. Summary of Experimental Investigations of H-T Exchange in NaF-NaBF4   

<table><tr><td>Run No.</td><td>Metal Coupons in Capsule</td><td>Procedure and Observations</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="2">3</td><td rowspan="2">Chromium</td><td>While heating to 550°C, vapor pressure in silica vessel rose and reached a steady value (4 mm) in 2 hr. Gas sample taken at this point showed 62 vol % H2. Removal of subsequent gas samples led to decrease in pressure, but with increases in percentage of H2. Gases were analyzed chromatographically. Assuming that all hydrogen came from the reaction</td></tr><tr><td>Cr + 3/2H2O + BF3(1) → CrF3 + 1/2B2O3(1) + 3/2H2(g), then, from analysis, 50 ppm H2O had reacted.</td></tr><tr><td>4</td><td>Hastelloy N</td><td>In heating to 530°C, vapor pressure in silica vessel rose steadily to 3.4 mm in 4 hr. First gas sample contained 74% H2; subsequent gas samples contained a higher percentage of H2, reached 87 vol%. Assuming all hydrogen gas came from reaction of H2O with Cr (see above) then 57 ppm H2O has reacted.</td></tr><tr><td>5</td><td>Chromium</td><td>Heated to 500°C; in 1.7 hr pressure reached a steady value of 3.5 mm, of which 74 vol % was H2. Subsequent samples were higher in H2. From hydrogen analyses, 58 ppm H2O had reacted. After 3.5 days at temperature, vessel and sample were cooled to room temperature and vessel and manifold were evacuated. Then 0.098 Ci of tritium gas (T2) was introduced into the silica vessel, which was heated back up to 500°C. The T2 interacted with the sample at 500° for almost three days. After cooling, capsule was cut open and some salt removed for tritium analysis. Another salt sample was removed after overnight evacuation. Both samples gave precisely the same low tritium concentration - 1.61 × 10-8g-atom of T per mole of salt. The total tritium in the salt sample was 0.00032 Ci.</td></tr><tr><td>6</td><td>Nickel</td><td>Heated to 520°C. Pressure reached steady value in 2 hr; first gas sample contained 64% H2. Experiment in progress.</td></tr></table>

exchanged with hydrogen in the salt. At the time the tritium was introduced into the experiment the concentration of bound hydrogen was miniscule - when expressed in terms of $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$ , the concentration was probably less than 1 ppm. (An attempt to analyze gaseous $\mathbf{H}_2$ , HT, and $\mathbf{T}_2$ , mass spectrographically, towards the end of the experiment was unsuccessful; hence the equilibrium ratio of bound hydrogen to bound tritium in the salt is not known.) The results of these experiments seem to indicate that in the presence of chromium the fluoroborate coolant salt does not retain sufficient $\mathbf{H}_2\mathbf{O}$ or $\mathrm{OH}^-$ ions to serve as the tritium trap in a molten-salt reactor.

The question arises: If the salt contained 120 ppm $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$ , how is it that in runs 3, 4, and 5 only 50 to 58 ppm $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$ can be accounted for in terms of the gas chromatographic analysis for hydrogen? The only answer consistent with the analysis for tritium in the salt at the end of run 5 is that the analysis of 120 ppm was too high by about a factor of 2. Since it is known that $\mathrm{C}_5\mathrm{H}_5\mathrm{N} - \mathrm{BF}_3$ is more stable than $\mathrm{NaF} - \mathrm{BF}_3$ , it is quite plausible that $\mathrm{C}_5\mathrm{H}_5\mathrm{N}$ (pyridine) would react with any

$$
\mathrm {N a F} - \mathrm {B} \underset {\mathrm {F}} {\rightleftharpoons} \mathrm {O}
$$

in the salt to form

$$
\mathrm {C} _ {5} \mathrm {H} _ {5} \mathrm {N} - \mathrm {B} \underset {\mathrm {F}} {\rightleftharpoons} 0,
$$

some of which would be distilled into the Karl Fischer reagent. (The

$$
\mathrm {C} _ {5} \mathrm {H} _ {5} \mathrm {N} - \mathrm {B} \stackrel {\mathrm {O}} {<  } _ {\mathrm {F}}
$$

would react with methanol in the Karl Fischer reagent to yield water, which would, of course, be reported as water.)

# 9.3 REACTION OF WATER WITH THE SODIUM FLUORIDE-SODIUM TETRAFLUOROBORATE EUTECTIC

Harold W. Kohn

On the basis of present knowledge,6 the equilibria in the $\mathrm{NaBF_4 - H_2O}$ system can be represented by the sum and difference of the two equations:

$$
\mathrm {N a B F} _ {4} + \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O} \rightleftharpoons \mathrm {N a B F} _ {3} \mathrm {O H} + \mathrm {H F}, \tag {1}
$$

$$
\mathrm {N a B F} _ {3} \mathrm {O H} \rightleftharpoons \mathrm {N a B F} _ {2} \mathrm {O} + \mathrm {H F}. \tag {2}
$$

A study of these equilibria was initiated in order to assess the hydroxide concentration attainable in molten fluoroborates and to evaluate the possibility of using the reverse reaction as a purification method. Neither the actual existence nor stability of $\mathrm{NaBF}_3\mathrm{OH}$ in molten fluoroborates has been established. On the basis of standard free energy data and estimates of B-OH bond energy (from the standard free energy of formation of boric acid suitably corrected for temperature) one concludes that the standard free energy is $+16.5$ kcal for the first reaction and $-26.5$ kcal for the second. These calculations suggest that the compound $\mathrm{NaBF}_3\mathrm{OH}$ is unstable with respect to decomposition into $\mathrm{NaBF}_2\mathrm{O}$ and HF. There are additional reasons, some presented later in this report, which lead to the inference that $\mathrm{NaBF}_3\mathrm{OH}$ is to some extent stable, especially when dilute in molten $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ .

By passing $\mathrm{HF} + \mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$ into $5\mathrm{kg}$ of a sodium fluoroborate eutectic in a nickel pot containing residual oxide and/or hydroxide, we attempted to measure the equilibrium quotient for the reverse of the two reactions shown. The concentration of $\mathrm{HF} + \mathrm{BF}_3$ in the off-gas stream was determined by alkalimetry, $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$ by titration with Karl Fischer reagent, and oxide plus hydroxide in the salt by $\mathrm{KBrF_4}$ reaction with filtered samples. It may be assumed that all the oxygen as measured is present as hydroxide, as oxide, or as a mixture of the two. If one assumes that reaction (1) predominates (oxygen present as hydroxide), the measured equilibrium quotients,

$$
K _ {1} = \frac {\mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O}}{\left[ \mathrm {O H} ^ {-} \right] \left[ \mathrm {H F} \right]},
$$

are 0.79 at $510^{\circ}$ , 1.3 at $460^{\circ}$ , 2.1 at $425^{\circ}$ , and 4.2 at $402^{\circ}$ . If one assumes reaction (2) predominates, the measured equilibrium quotients,

$$
K _ {2} = \frac {\left[ \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O} \right]}{\left[ \mathrm {O} ^ {2 -} \right] \left[ \mathrm {H F} \right] ^ {2}},
$$

are 120 at $510^{\circ}$ , 145 at $450^{\circ}$ , 240 at $425^{\circ}$ , and 605 at $402^{\circ}$ C. The values obtained at $402^{\circ}$ and $510^{\circ}$ are single determinations. The variation in individual determinations is quite wide because of various analytical

difficulties; the interference of $\mathbf{BF}_3$ with the Karl Fischer titration is particularly serious. Hence the data are not useful for calculations involving the coexistence of oxide and hydroxide.

In a continued effort to examine the pertinence of reactions (1) and (2), auxiliary experiments were conducted adding various quantities of boron oxide to the melt. In one experiment $3\mathrm{g}$ of $\mathrm{B}_2\mathrm{O}_3$ was added to the melt, and after overnight agitation the melt was purged continuously with a gas stream containing HF. For 1 hr the water in the effluent gas remained low, $<0.2\mathrm{mg}/\mathrm{liter}$ , then suddenly increased about tenfold to $2\mathrm{mg}/\mathrm{liter}$ . We assumed that this was due to the preferential reaction of oxide first with HF to form hydroxide followed by the reaction of hydroxide with HF to form water. On the other hand, when we measured $\mathrm{HF/H_2O}$ over a range in a highly ( $>1000\mathrm{ppm}$ ) oxide-contaminated melt, so that the oxide is sensibly constant, the results shown in Table 9.3 were obtained. These data suggest that the true equilibrium quotient is more likely to be

$$
\frac {\left[ \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O} \right]}{\left[ \mathrm {H F} \right] ^ {2} \left[ \mathrm {O} ^ {2 -} \right]}
$$

rather than

$$
\frac {[ \mathrm {H} _ {2} \mathrm {O} ]}{[ \mathrm {H F} ] [ \mathrm {O H} ^ {-} ]}.
$$

When we passed $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$ through molten $\mathrm{NaF - NaBF_4}$ eutectic to see whether the reverse of reaction (1) or (2) was the preferred reaction by measuring the equivalence of HF per molecule, the results were too erratic to be meaningful. Again, interference from $\mathrm{BF}_3$ is an important factor.

We also conducted experiments using deuterium as a tracer to test the stability of the $\mathrm{NaBF}_3\mathrm{OD}$ formed and to avoid interferences from this ubiquitous $\mathbf{H}_2\mathbf{O}$ which could arise from accidental contamination. A gas stream containing $\mathrm{D}_2\mathrm{O}$ vapor was passed into a $\mathrm{NaF - NaBF_4}$

Table 9.3. Concentration of $\mathrm{{HF}}/{\mathrm{H}}_{2}\mathrm{O}$ in Equilibrium with NaF-NaBF ${}_{4}$   

<table><tr><td>HF (meq/liter)</td><td>H2O (meq/liter)</td><td>H2O/HF</td><td>H2O/(HF)2</td></tr><tr><td>5.0</td><td>0.022</td><td>4.5 × 10-2</td><td>8.8 × 10-4</td></tr><tr><td>6.2</td><td>0.035</td><td>6.4</td><td>9.3</td></tr><tr><td>8.5</td><td>0.083</td><td>9.8</td><td>11.4</td></tr></table>

eutectic melt at 400 and $550^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . The amount of deuterium which remained in the salt was determined by reaction with metallic chromium in a sealed capsule, $^{8}$

$$
3 \mathrm {N a B F} _ {3} \mathrm {O D} + \mathrm {C r} ^ {0} \rightarrow \mathrm {C r F} _ {3} + 3 \mathrm {N a B F} _ {2} \mathrm {O} + 3 / _ {2} \mathrm {D} _ {2}. \tag {3}
$$

The hydrogen gas generated in the reaction was purified in the process by diffusion through the sealed capsule. Most of the gas so collected was protium, corresponding to 300 to $500~\mathrm{ppm}~\mathrm{OH}^{-}$ which, presumably, remained from the previous experiment. A small amount might also have been contributed by a handling blank. A nonnegligible amount of deuterium was, however, retained by the salt and released by reaction (3), but this was less than $5\%$ of the total deuterium added. The results are summarized in Table 9.4.

It was anticipated that half of the added $\mathbf{D}_2\mathbf{O}$ would be lost immediately as DF by reaction (1), and an additional amount might be lost as a result of corrosion reactions. The apparent retention of a small fraction of deuterium leaves open the possibility that a small amount of hydrogenous species can be retained in sodium fluoroborate at $400^{\circ}$ . The release of protium in this experiment, corresponding to results obtained by Cantor and Waller, $^8$ suggests rather an artifact effect.

An auxiliary observation we have made is that in spite of the addition of comparatively large amounts of $\mathbf{B}_2\mathbf{O}_3$ and of water to the fluoroborate melts, none of the filtered samples contained greater than 816 ppm of $\mathrm{O}^{2-}$ , although, according to inventory calculations, the concentration could have been as high as 2500 ppm. This result suggests the separation of an oxide-rich phase which has been reported cursorily before $^{9,10}$ but the existence of which Pawlenko disputes. $^6$ Indeed, if

we use a saturation value for oxide of 816 ppm to calculate the equilibrium quotients from the data in Table 9.4, we get, respectively, $K_{1} = 0.85$ , 1.2, and 1.85 and $K_{1} \cdot K_{2} = 166$ , 175, and 215, in reasonable agreement with the previous $460^{\circ}$ data. Analysis of the samples has also indicated segregation of an oxygen-rich phase during cooling of fluoroborate melts, suggesting that solubility of oxides in these melts is quite limited.[11]

# 9.4 RAMAN SPECTRA OF MOLTEN $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ AND NaF-NaBF $_4$ TO $606^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$

Arvin S. Quist John B. Bates George E. Boyd

Studies of the vibrational spectra of molten salts provide the possibility of directly identifying polyatomic species which may exist in the melt and also indicating the extent and nature of interionic interactions in these liquids. Differences in the vibrational spectra of an ion observed in the "free" and the molten state may be attributed to cation-anion complex formation in the latter or to interactions with quasi-lattice states in the melt, depending on the magnitude and type of the changes observed.

Raman spectroscopy is particularly well suited for studying the tetrahedral tetrafluoroborate anion $\left(\mathrm{BF}_4\right)$ , because all four normal modes of vibration are Raman active. The characteristic vibrational spectrum of the $\mathrm{BF}_4$ ion in molten salts has not been obtained until now, primarily because these melts are very corrosive toward the usual optical window materials. However, the recently developed windowless cells for laser Raman spectroscopy of fused salts<sup>1,2</sup> can be used to study these melts.

Table 9.4. Concentration of ${\mathrm{D}}_{2}/{\mathrm{D}}_{2}\mathrm{O}$ in Equilibrium with $\mathrm{{NaF}} - {\mathrm{{NaBF}}}_{4}$   

<table><tr><td>Sample No.</td><td>Temperature of Preparation</td><td>Milliequivalents of D2O Added per Gram</td><td>Milliequivalents of D2O Formed per Gram</td><td>D2/D2O</td></tr><tr><td>15</td><td>400</td><td>9.65</td><td>0.33</td><td>0.034</td></tr><tr><td>16</td><td>400</td><td>28.2</td><td>1.11</td><td>0.039</td></tr><tr><td>17</td><td>550</td><td>3.86</td><td>0.11</td><td>0.029</td></tr><tr><td>18</td><td>400</td><td>16.2</td><td>0.91</td><td>0.056</td></tr><tr><td>19</td><td>400</td><td>#18 exchanged with H2 gas</td><td>0.42(?)</td><td></td></tr></table>

High-purity $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ was obtained from L. O. Gilpatrick of the Reactor Chemistry Division. $^{13}$ Single-crystal NaF was purchased from Harshaw Chemical Co., Cleveland, Ohio. The high-temperature Raman studies were conducted with the salts contained in nickel windowless cells, $^{12}$ which in turn were enclosed in sealed quartz tubes under helium at a pressure of approximately $1/3$ atm. There was no direct contact between the molten salt and the quartz. A cutaway view of the furnace used is given in Fig. 9.2. A complete

$^{13}$ L. O. Gilpatrick and R. Apple, MSR Program Semiann. Progr. Rept. Feb. 28, 1970, ORNL-4548, p. 134.

description is given elsewhere. $^{1,2}$ Raman spectra were recorded photoelectrically with a Jarrell-Ash model 25-300 spectrometer (Jarrell-Ash Company, Waltham, Massachusetts). The 4880 Å line of a Spectra-Physics 141 argon ion laser (Spectra-Physics, Mountain View, California) was used to excite the spectra. The scattered light was collected at right angles to the incident beam with the optical system described previously. $^{1,2}$

The Raman spectrum of molten $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ was obtained at temperatures from $414^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ (just above its melting point of $408^{\circ}$ ) to $606^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . A typical spectrum is shown in Fig. 9.3 for a temperature of $437^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . Spectra of the eutectic mixture (mp $384^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ) of 8 mole $\%$ NaF in

Fig. 9.2. Furnace for Laser Raman Spectroscopy of Molten Salts.

![](images/a159e6ee1d3ef644bffec81cdabb8f7dae96dc0fdc7cc38d00aa9bf6f8a62a75.jpg)  
Fig. 9.3. A Typical Raman Spectrum of $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ .

$\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ were also recorded to temperatures of $503^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . The spectrum of the mixture did not differ significantly from that for pure $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ at corresponding temperatures. The Raman spectra of polycrystalline $\mathrm{NaBF}_4^{14}$ and of a $3M$ aqueous solution of $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ also were measured at $25^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ in connection with the studies on molten $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ . The $\mathrm{BF}_4^-$ frequencies observed in the Raman spectra of polycrystalline, aqueous $3M$ , and molten $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ are collected in Table 9.5.

The four fundamental modes of vibration of the $\mathrm{BF}_4^-$ ion have been assigned for aqueous $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ solutions. In the melt at $483^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ the totally symmetric stretch, $\nu_{1}(A_{1})$ , was observed as a strong polarized band at 774 $\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ , and the degenerate bending modes, $\nu_{2}(E)$ and $\nu_{4}(F_{2})$ , appeared at 358 and $532~\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ respectively. The asymmetric stretching mode, $\nu_{3}(F_{2})$ , was observed as a weak band at approximately $1065~\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ . These assignments are consistent with those by Goubeau and Bues. The frequencies of these bands in the melt did not change significantly with added NaF. However, their maxima appeared to shift to lower frequencies with increasing temperature.

The only unusual feature of the spectrum shown in Fig. 9.3 is the weak band which appears on the low-frequency side of $\nu_{1}$ at approximately 710 to 720 $\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ . With increasing temperature this band increased in intensity, and another, weaker band appeared near $660~\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ . These two weak bands are most probably caused by impurities, in particular by $\mathrm{SiF}_5^-$ and $\mathrm{SiF}_6^{2-}$ ions, which may be present as a result of the reaction of

Table 9.5. Frequencies Observed in the Raman Spectra of Polycrystalline, Aqueous $3M$ , and Molten $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ S = strong, M = medium, W = weak, P = polarized   

<table><tr><td>Aqueous, 3 M, 25°C</td><td>Molten, 483°C</td><td>Polycrystalline, 25°C</td></tr><tr><td>357 M</td><td>358 M</td><td>344 M v2(E) 369 M</td></tr><tr><td>528 M</td><td>530 M</td><td>532 M v4(F2) 534 M 554 M</td></tr><tr><td>773 S, P</td><td>774 S, P</td><td>785 S v1(A1)</td></tr><tr><td>1080 W</td><td>1065 W</td><td>1115 W v3(F2) 1055 W</td></tr></table>

$\mathbf{BF}_3$ (a result of the partial dissociation of $\mathbf{BF}_4^-$ at high temperatures) with quartz to form volatile $\mathrm{SiF_4}$ which in turn dissolved in the melt and reacted with the excess fluoride ion present. These bands are polarized, and their wave numbers are consistent with those for the totally symmetric stretching modes previously reported for $\mathrm{SiF}_5^-$ (ref. 16) and $\mathrm{SiF}_6^{2 - }$ (ref. 17).

From a study of the polarized infrared and Raman spectra of single-crystal $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ ,<sup>18</sup> the correlation field components observed in the polycrystalline spectra

reported earlier $^{1,4}$ were assigned, and the observed band splitting was discussed in terms of the anistropic forces acting on the $\mathrm{BF}_4^-$ ion in the crystal lattice. The observed wave number difference between the high-frequency components of the $\mathrm{BF}_4^-$ fundamentals in crystalline $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ and those observed in the melt (Table 9.5) can be attributed to anisotropy at the site occupied by $\mathrm{BF}_4^-$ in the crystal lattice. The lack of any evidence for splitting of the bands in molten $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ (i.e., as separate components or as shoulders on the main bands) indicates that there is no cation-anion complex formation; the $\mathrm{BF}_4^-$ ion in the melt is in an isotropic environment. This conclusion is supported by the close agreement between the spectrum of the $3M$ aqueous solution and the melt spectra (Table 9.5), because the spectrum in solution can be considered as arising from the "free ion."

In general the band maxima in molten $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ appeared to shift to lower frequencies with increasing temperature. For example, the maximum intensity of the $\nu_{1}$ band was observed at $776\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ at $414^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , while at $606^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ its frequency was $772\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ . The shift to lower frequencies of the $\mathrm{BF}_4^-$ vibrations as the temperature of the melt is increased may be due to the increased population of higher vibrational levels or to changes in the effective force constants of $\mathrm{BF}_4^-$ resulting from changes in cation-anion interactions as the temperature is changed. It should also be pointed out that, similar to the behavior of molten perchlorates, $^{19,20}$ no low-frequency, quasi-lattice vibrations of the type common to nitrate melts were observed in molten $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ .

# 9.5 ELECTRONIC POLARIZABILITY OF THE TETRAFLUOROBORATE ION AND VARIOUS GASEOUS HALIDE MOLECULES AS COMPARED WITH THE FREE (GASEOUS) HALIDE IONS

M. A. Bredig

Together with the polarizing power, mainly of the positive ion, the polarizability of the negative ion is one of the important fundamental parameters determining the chemical and physical properties of a salt. For example, the large difference in the polarizabilities of the $\mathbf{Cl}^{-}$ and $\mathbf{F}^{-}$ ions is implicitly the basis of the metal transfer process using a chloride melt for purposes of

separations presently under development. Unfortunately, there is considerable confusion in the literature in regard to the use of proper standard ion polarizabilities with which to compare the measured polarizabilities of a given salt. We are dealing in the following with a brief discussion of this problem as applied to (1) the tetrafluoroborate ion, of great current interest to the Molten-Salt Reactor Program, (2) a number of halides of polyvalent elements similar to boron, and (3) some "anomalous" behavior found with tri- and tetrahalides of the larger, more highly polarizable halide ions, $\mathbf{Br}^{-}$ and $\mathbf{I}^{-}$ .

# 9.5.1 The Polarizability of the Tetrafluoroborate Ion

The polarizabilities, $\alpha_{m}$ , of the alkali metal tetrafluoroborates, $\mathbf{MBF}_4$ , measured by Cantor, McDermott, and Gilpatrick $^{21}$ were used together with the polarizabilities of the free (gaseous) cations, $\alpha_{+}$ , given by Fajans and co-workers, $^{22}$ to derive $\alpha_{-} \equiv \alpha_{m} - \alpha_{+}$ for $\mathbf{BF}_4$ in these crystals (Table 9.6).

In the salts of sodium to cesium, $\alpha_{-} = 3.06 \pm 0.04\mathring{\mathrm{A}}^{3}$ does not show a systematic change. This is as expected, because in $\mathbf{M}(\mathbf{BF}_4)$ the weak field of $\mathbf{M}^{+}$ ("ionic potential," $z / r \approx 1.05$ to 0.6 as compared with $z / r \approx 15$ for $\mathbf{B}^{3 + }$ ) contributes very little to the tightening of the electron shells, which is $\Delta \alpha / 4 = -0.84 / 4 = -0.21\mathring{\mathrm{A}}^{3}$ (21%) per $\mathbf{F}^{-}$ ion. In the alkali sulfates, $\Delta \alpha = -1.40 \pm 0.03\mathring{\mathrm{A}}^{3}$ per $\mathbf{O}^{2 - }$ and $\Delta \alpha / \alpha = 49\%$ are similarly constant.

An apparent trend for $\alpha_{-}$ , from $2.87\mathring{\mathrm{A}}^{3}$ in $\mathrm{Na(BF_4)}$ to 2.29 in $\mathrm{Cs(BF_4)}$ , reported earlier,[21] was merely the result of the very misleading nature of the average solid-state polarizabilities proposed for the cations by Tessman, Kahn, and Shockley[23] which were used in the calculations and which are inconsistent with the observation of distinct systematic deviations from additivity in the alkali halides.

The polarizability of $\mathrm{BF}_4^-$ in the lithium salt, $\alpha_{-} < 3.4$ Å $^3$ (see Table 9.6), is not accessible at present to more exact measurement but most likely resembles that of the other four salts, $\alpha_{-} \approx 3.0$ Å $^3$ .

It seems noteworthy that the amount of the tightening in $\mathbf{BF}_4^-$ , $-0.21\mathring{\mathrm{A}}^3$ per $\mathbf{F}^{-}$ , appears to be not smaller, as might have been expected from the larger

Table 9.6. Fluoride Polarizability in $\mathbf{BF}_4^-$ (Crystals) and Free $\mathbf{F}^-$
