B-F distance, $^{24}$ 1.45 vs $1.30\text{\AA},^{25}$ but slightly greater than in gaseous $\mathrm{BF}_3$ $-0.18\text{\AA}^3$ as derived from $\alpha_{m} = 2.38\text{\AA}^3$ .

# 9.5.2 Test of a Recent Theoretical Set of Ion Polarizabilities

In considering the tightening of free fluoride ions in the strong field of $\mathbf{B}^{3+}$ , I have used $\alpha(\mathbf{F}^{-}) = 0.97\mathring{\mathrm{A}}^{3}$ ( $\lambda = 5897\mathring{\mathrm{A}}$ ) and $0.95\mathring{\mathrm{A}}^{3}$ ( $\lambda = \infty$ ) from the set of free ion polarizabilities of Fajans and co-workers.[22] Recently, on the basis of a theoretical model, speciously documented, a radical modification was proposed to a much larger value, $1.56\mathring{\mathrm{A}}^{3}$ (model II, Wilson and Curtis[27]) or even higher, $1.83\mathring{\mathrm{A}}^{3}$ (their model I). For a test of the validity of the value 1.56, I have chosen as a simple criterion the relative change in polarizability, $\Delta \alpha/\alpha$ (or refractivity, $\Delta R_{\infty}/R_{\infty}$ , at infinite wavelength), on formation of the hydrogen halides from the gaseous ions according to $\mathbf{X}^{-} + \mathbf{H}^{+} \rightarrow \mathbf{HX}$ . $R_{\mathrm{D}}$ of liquid hydrogen fluoride has recently been measured by Perkins,[28] and it is well known for both liquid and gaseous HCl, HBr, and HI, the differences between the two states being small, especially when corrections of

old literature values $^{26}$ are made for more accurate density data.

Figure 9.4 compares $\Delta \alpha / \alpha = (\alpha_{\mathbf{H} \mathbf{X}} - \alpha_{\mathbf{X}^{-}}) / \alpha_{\mathbf{X}^{-}}$ as a function of $\alpha_{\mathbf{X}^{-}}$ for the newly proposed WC II and one of the commonly accepted sets of polarizabilities

![](images/3404c41f6d77d150ea46aeb3771baa6940b10820af344a5cb28a510de9f2196e.jpg)  
Fig. 9.4. Test of Wilson-Curtis Model II (1970) vs Fajans Free Halide Ion Polarizabilities $(\lambda = \infty)$ . Relative change in polarizability for the process $\mathbf{X}^{-} + \mathbf{H}^{+} \rightarrow \mathbf{HX}$ . Intercept $-\Delta \alpha / \alpha = 0.48$ for $\alpha = 0$ is unreasonable.

(Fajans, F) of the four halide ions. Curve WC II exhibits an entirely unreasonable dependence of $\Delta \alpha / \alpha$ on $\alpha$ , namely, an increase, instead of a decrease with decreasing $\alpha$ from $X = Br$ on towards the large value of $\Delta \alpha / \alpha = 0.5$ for $\alpha = 0$ . In sharp contrast, curve F shows the expected decrease towards $\Delta \alpha / \alpha = 0$ for $\alpha = 0$ : on an infinitely rigid ion ( $\alpha = 0$ ) a proton cannot effect any change.

In still another test, on the corresponding $\Delta \alpha / \alpha$ values for the formation of the three boron trihalides $\mathrm{BF}_3$ , $\mathrm{BCl}_3$ , and $\mathrm{BBr}_3$ from $\mathrm{B}^{3+} + 3\mathrm{X}^{-} \rightarrow \mathrm{BX}_3$ , an unreasonable rise towards $\Delta \alpha / \alpha = 0.56$ for $\alpha = 0$ is again found for curve WC II in Fig. 9.5. Curve F does show the expected behavior. It is obviously justified, then, to conclude, as Fajans did by applying his method of inequalities of quotients,[29] that the recent theoretical free ion polarizabilities[27] are in disagreement with basic principles and experimental observations and that there is no reason to abandon the well-proven semiempirical set of these polarizabilities, especially that of $\mathrm{F}^{-}$ , $\alpha = 0.95\mathring{\mathrm{A}}^3$ .

29 K. Fajans, private communication; J. Phys. Chem. 74, 3407-10 (1970).

![](images/268deecc845e1945716b5b7b4dddb5c8a29892793d14748e162af71e458812f9.jpg)  
Fig. 9.5. Test of Wilson-Curtis Model II (1970) vs Fajans Free Halide Ion Polarizabilities $(\lambda = 589~\mathrm{nm})$ . Relative change in polarizability for the process $3X^{-} + B^{3+} \rightarrow BX_{3}$ . Intercept $-\Delta \alpha / \alpha = 0.56$ for $\alpha = 0$ is unreasonable.

# 9.5.3. Effect of Interaction Between Halide Ions on Polarizability

The values of $\Delta \alpha / \alpha$ for $\mathrm{BCl}_3$ and $\mathrm{BBr}_3$ in Fig. 9.5 represent a smaller tightening of $\mathrm{Cl}^-$ and $\mathrm{Br}^-$ than expected from that of $\mathrm{F}^-$ in $\mathrm{BF}_3$ (dashed curve). I propose as an explanation a mutual interaction, such as a "loosening" of the larger, more highly polarizable halide ions $\mathrm{Br}^-$ and $\mathrm{I}^-$ , when "crowded" around a small ion such as $\mathrm{B}^{3+}$ . This interpretation applies also to a similar experimental observation reported for $\mathrm{Al}_2\mathrm{I}_6$ and to a smaller degree for $\mathrm{Al}_2\mathrm{Br}_6$ in the series of the three aluminum trihalide dimer molecules.[30-32] Here the tightening of $\mathrm{I}^-$ , $-\Delta \alpha / \alpha = 0.089$ , and also that of $\mathrm{Br}^-$ , $-\Delta \alpha / \alpha = 0.144$ , is smaller than expected in comparison with that of $\mathrm{Cl}^-$ , $-\Delta \alpha / \alpha = 0.153$ (Fig. 9.6). Quite similarly, in the carbon and silicon tetrahalides, $\mathrm{CX}_4$ and $\mathrm{SiX}_4$ , the apparent tightening increases at first from $-\Delta \alpha / \alpha = 0.24$ and 0.18 in the fluorides to 0.26 and 0.21 in the chlorides but then decreases to 0.24 and 0.19 in the bromides. By analogy with $\mathrm{Al}_2\mathrm{I}_6$ a further decrease must surely be anticipated for the iodides, for which data do not seem to be available.

The mutual interaction of the halide ions here referred to may consist of a mutual loosening of the electron shells near the periphery of the molecule.

30K.Fajans,Z.Phys.Chem.B24,103-51 (1934).   
31M. A. Bredig and F. K. V. Koch, Z. Phys. Chem. B24, 187-93 (1934).   
$^{32}$ F. K. V. Koch and H. Kohner. Z. Phys. Chem. B 24, 194-98 (1934).

![](images/95c658a273a9402af22d0602e190fb8ac4b5cd36631a5771e213c96beb41a340.jpg)  
Fig. 9.6. Effect of "Crowding" of $\mathbf{X}^{-}$ on Polarizability in Aluminum Trihalides and Carbon or Silicon Tetrahalides ( $\lambda = 589\mathrm{nm}$ ).

Alternatively, and perhaps more plausibly, the relative tightening of $\mathbf{Br}^{-}$ and $\mathbf{I}^{-}$ in the central fields of $\mathbf{B}^{3+}$ , $\mathbf{C}^{4+}$ , $\mathbf{Al}^{3+}$ , and $\mathbf{Si}^{4+}$ may just not quite proceed as far as it could in a molecule with fewer, or smaller, less

"crowded" $\mathbf{X}^{-}$ ions such as in a $\mathbf{BX}_2^+$ or in the fluorides of these elements. Attempts to understand this significant and quite distinct effect theoretically in a more quantitative manner would seem to be of interest.

# 10. Physical Chemistry of Molten Salts

# 10.1 LIQUIDUS TEMPERATURE OF THE SALT MIXTURE LiF-BeF $_2$ -ThF $_4$ (70-15-15 MOLE %)

L. O. Gilpatrick C. J. Barton H. Insley

Bauman and Chang<sup>1</sup> have recently recommended that the salt composition $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2 - ThF_4}$ (70-15-15 mole %) be adopted as the optimum composition for succeeding studies of the permanent-core single-fluid MSBR. In view of its probable importance in future reactor design considerations, we thought it desirable to confirm the liquidus temperature and the primary phase assignment of this specific composition.

The composition was prepared from the pure components and examined by the quench tube technique. It was found to have a melting point of $505 \pm 2^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , and the primary phase was identified from its optical properties as $3\mathrm{LiF} \cdot \mathrm{ThF}_4$ . The liquidus temperature was confirmed by an independent set of three differential thermal analysis (D.T.A.) measurements which averaged $506.3 \pm 1^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ .

Results agree very well with the published phase diagram in all respects.2

# 10.2 EQUILIBRIUM PHASE RELATIONSHIPS IN THE SYSTEM LiF-BeF $_2$ -CeF $_3$

L. O. Gilpatrick H. Insley C. J. Barton

Investigation of this system was reported earlier and has progressed far enough to warrant the preparation of a tentative phase diagram (Fig. 10.1). Additional data have been obtained using the gradient quench technique. The solubility of $\mathbf{C e F_{3}}$ in LiF-BeF2 compositions was measured earlier and correlated with other

$\mathbf{CeF}_3$ solubility measurements.5 Interest in the system stems chiefly from the analogy between the behavior of $\mathbf{CeF}_3$ and $\mathbf{PuF}_3$ , a possible fuel constituent.7

Four primary phase fields have been identified in this system, of which the largest is $\mathrm{CeF}_3$ , as expected from its high melting point $(1459^{\circ}\mathrm{C})$ . Lithium fluoride forms the next most extensive primary phase field, followed by $2\mathrm{LiF} \cdot \mathrm{BeF}_2$ . Adjoining or contained in this field are the lowest-melting compositions in the system, the lowest being the ternary eutectic melting at $358^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ having the approximate composition $\mathrm{LiF - BeF}_2 - \mathrm{CeF}_3$ (47.5-52.0-0.5 mole%). As $\mathrm{CeF}_3$ is added to a mixture of this composition there is a sharp rise in the liquidus temperature. This can be seen from the closely spaced isothermal contours tracing the liquidus boundary in Fig. 10.1. For example, when as little as 3 mole% $\mathrm{CeF}_3$ is added to the LiF-BeF $_2$ eutectic composition melting at $360^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , the equilibrium liquidus temperature rises to $635^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ .

At higher $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ compositions, such as those above 80 mole $\%$ , the experimental difficulties entailed in securing equilibrium data mount rapidly because of the high viscosity of liquid $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ . Additional data will be needed to refine the present diagram at high $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ compositions. At the higher $\mathrm{CeF_3}$ compositions, the need for high-temperature techniques has been a limitation.

Invariant compositions and their temperatures can be read from Fig. 10.1. They represent tentative values based upon our present understanding of the system.

# 10.3 PHASE RELATIONSHIPS IN THE SYSTEM $\mathbf{C e F}_{3}$ -ThF4

L. O. Gilpatrick H. Insley C. J. Barton

The system $\mathbf{CeF}_3\cdot \mathbf{ThF}_4$ is being studied as part of a long-range investigation of the system LiF-BeF2-CeF3

![](images/0c496d4281c550a5560b472f3d7b49a6cb8d299ca50e4cb3b9620d406e1bd26c.jpg)  
Fig. 10.1. The System LiF-BeF $_2$ -CeF $_3$ .

$\mathbf{ThF_4}$ . Preliminary results were given for this subsystem in an earlier report. Some progress has been made in overcoming the experimental problems referred to earlier when using the quench-tube technique at temperatures between 1000 and $1200^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . Inserting a graphite liner with a cap into the nickel furnace core has prevented the nickel-to-nickel welding between sample tube and core which previously resulted in many lost heats. Use of Rodar alloy (Fe-Ni-Co-Mn, 51-29-17-3 at. $\%$ quench tubes which have a greater strength at $1200^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ than pure nickel has also reduced the failure rate, although neither it nor other commonly available alloys are found to possess both entirely adequate strength and corrosion resistance in this temperature range.

A preliminary phase diagram is presented in Fig. 10.2 which represents our present understanding of this system. The compositions region greater than 40 mole $\%$ in $\mathrm{ThF_4}$ has been examined in sufficient detail at

temperatures below $1100^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ to give a fairly clear picture of the system in this region. A eutectic is formed at 45 mole $\%$ $\mathrm{ThF_4}$ between $\mathrm{CeF_3\cdot 2ThF_4}$ and $\mathrm{ThF_4}$ ; it exhibits the lowest melting temperature in the system, estimated to be $950^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . Thorium fluoride is the primary phase at higher $\mathrm{ThF_4}$ compositions, but the compound $\mathrm{CeF_3\cdot 2ThF_4}$ is the primary phase for only a narrow composition range from the eutectic to 73 mole $\%$ $\mathrm{ThF_4}$ . At this composition a peritectic is found which is formed by the incongruent melting of $\mathrm{CeF_3\cdot 2ThF_4}$ at $960^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ .

A second peritectic is formed when the compound $\mathrm{CeF}_3\cdot \mathrm{ThF}_4$ melts incongruently at about $1155^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . At higher temperatures the only stable solid phase is $\mathrm{CeF}_3$ solid solution. From the change in optical properties, we infer the existence of a $\mathrm{CeF}_3$ solid solution; but more work is needed to define the extent of this region as shown by the dotted lines. Below approximately $860^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ $\mathrm{CeF}_3\cdot 2\mathrm{ThF}_4$ is unstable, and only $\mathrm{ThF}_4$ and $\mathrm{CeF}_3$ solid solution exists. Additional information is needed especially in the temperature range above $1100^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ to complete this study.

![](images/e9d2af09a8503f67ae62d33a0e16ab45c86ebe6b371a349c9fee9ab23447f56c.jpg)  
Fig. 10.2. The System $\mathsf{CeF}_3\cdot \mathsf{ThF}_4$

# 10.4 THE INSTABILITY OF $\mathbf{PuOF}$ AND THE SOLUBILITY OF $\mathbf{Pu(III)}$ IN MSBR SOLVENT SALT

C.E.Bamberger R.G.Ross C.F.Baes, Jr.

We have previously reported<sup>9</sup> measurements of the following equilibrium involving the oxidative precipitation of plutonium oxide at 590 and $690^{\circ}$ :

$$
\begin{array}{l} 3 / 4 \mathrm {T h O} _ {2} (\mathrm {s s}) + 1 / 2 \mathrm {N i O} (\mathrm {c}) + \mathrm {P u F} _ {3} (\mathrm {d}) \\ \rightleftharpoons \mathrm {P u O} _ {2} (\mathrm {s s}) + 3 / 4 \mathrm {T h F} _ {4} (\mathrm {d}) + 1 / 2 \mathrm {N i} ^ {\circ} (\mathrm {c}), \\ \end{array}
$$

where ss, c, and d denote, respectively, a solid solution of $\mathrm{PuO_2}$ and $\mathrm{ThO_2}$ , pure crystalline phases, and components dissolved in LiF-BeF $_2$ -ThF $_4$ (72-16-12 mole%). From these results and available thermochemical data for the other reactants, we were able to estimate the free energy of formation of $\mathrm{PuF_3}$ and to conclude therefrom that under the normal reducing conditions of an MSBR fuel there was no possibility of precipitating plutonium as $\mathrm{PuO_2}$ , $\mathrm{PuO_{1.61}}$ , or $\mathrm{PuO_{1.5}}$ . One other oxygen-containing compound of $\mathrm{Pu(III)}$ has been reported by early workers<sup>10</sup> from x-ray examina

tion of impurities formed during the reduction of $\mathbf{PuF}_3$ by atomic hydrogen and during the determination of the melting points of $\mathbf{PuF}_3$ in an argon atmosphere. Indeed, Oetting<sup>11</sup> has estimated the thermochemical values for $\mathbf{PuOF}$ by comparison with those for $\mathbf{PuOCl}$ , $\mathbf{PuCl}_3$ , and $\mathbf{PuF}_3$ . Using his estimation procedure and our revised estimate for the formation free energy of $\mathbf{PuF}_3$ , we would predict the precipitation of $\mathbf{PuOF}$ by the addition of $\mathbf{ThO}_2$ to a molten fluoride containing $\mathbf{PuF}_3$ . This has been clearly ruled out, however, by one of our previously reported tests in which an addition of excess oxide failed to remove a detectable amount of $\mathbf{Pu(III)}$ from such a molten fluoride solution nearly saturated with $\mathbf{PuF}_3$ (0.21 mole % at $494^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ). Thus the $\mathbf{PuOF}$ clearly is less stable than might be judged from Oetting's estimation procedure.

The present measurements were made in order to confirm previous estimates of the solubility of $\mathrm{PuF_3}$ in LiF-BeF2-ThF4 (72-16-12 mole %) and to determine if the presence of oxide has any effect on that solubility. While it appears that the solubility is not decreased by the precipitation of an oxyfluoride, the possibility remains that it might be increased by the formation of an oxy ion such as $\mathrm{PuO^{+}}$ .

The experimental procedure consisted in equilibrating the melt with excess $\mathbf{PuF}_3$ (containing $\sim 0.1\%$ $^{241}\mathrm{Am}$ )

by sparging the mixture with argon at $120~\mathrm{cc / min}$ for several (4-16) hours. The plutonium concentration was determined by alpha pulse-height analysis of ${}^{239}\mathrm{Pu}$ and by neutron activation analysis. The ${}^{241}\mathrm{Am}$ present showed the same distribution behavior as ${}^{239}\mathrm{Pu}$ between the crystalline $\mathrm{PuF_3}$ and the melt and therefore was used as a tracer for rapid analysis during the runs. In runs with $\mathrm{ThO_2}$ present, stirring was used for mixing the phases under an atmosphere of $\mathrm{Ar + 4\% H_2}$ . The reducing atmosphere was used in order to avoid the formation of $\mathrm{PuO_2 - ThO_2}$ solid solution in case of accidental contamination with air.

The solubility of $\mathrm{PuF_3}$ measured in the presence and absence of $\mathrm{ThO_2}$ was the same within the scatter of the analytical data (Fig. 10.3). This indicates that an oxy ion of plutonium III, such as $\mathrm{PuO^{+}}$ , has no significant stability in the molten fluoride solvent used. Thus $\mathrm{PuO^{+}}$ appears to form neither a stable solid fluoride nor a stable species in molten fluoride.

The results of the various analyses used to determine the solubility of $\mathrm{PuF_3}$ in 72-16-12 salt (Fig. 10.3) were fitted by least squares, giving

![](images/76c3d626fc6e446e68f0afa07a62ca50f3de770c702726e720ee8d013d318027.jpg)  
Fig. 10.3. Solubility of $\mathbf{PuF}_3$ in LiF-BeF $_2$ -ThF $_4$ (72-16-12 Mole%).

$$
\begin{array}{l} \log S _ {\mathrm {P u F} _ {3}} (\text {mole} \%) \\ = (3. 0 1 \pm 0. 0 6) - (2. 4 1 \pm 0. 0 5) \times 1 0 ^ {3} / T. \\ \end{array}
$$

The heat of solution of $\mathbf{PuF}_3$ was estimated from this equation to be

$$
\Delta H _ {s} = 1 1, 0 0 8 \pm 2 3 7 \mathrm {c a l / m o l e}.
$$

These results are nearly identical with those obtained by Barton et al.12 for $\mathrm{CeF}_3$ in the same solvent salt, 72-16-12, thus confirming the suitability of $\mathrm{CeF}_3$ as a proxy for plutonium in the earlier measurements.

# 10.5 OXIDE CHEMISTRY OF PROTACTINIUM IN MSBR FUEL SOLVENT SALT

R.G.Ross C.E.Bamberger C.F.Baes, Jr.

The precipitation of protactinium from $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2}$ $\mathrm{ThF_4}$ molten mixtures by oxide additions was demonstrated by Shaffer et al. some years ago.13 When it later became apparent from reductive extraction measurements that the valence of protactinium in such melts was $+4$ , it was generally assumed that the oxide precipitated in these earlier experiments was $\mathrm{PaO}_2$ Furthermore, since $\mathrm{UO}_2$ and $\mathrm{ThO}_2$ form solid solutions in equilibrium with such melts when $\mathrm{UF_4}$ is present,14 it seemed likely that protactinium was precipitated by the formation of a (U-Pa-Th)O2 solid solution.

The present further investigation of the oxide chemistry of protactinium in molten fluorides was undertaken by us to identify the oxide phases of protactinium which are precipitated under various conditions and to measure the related equilibria. In an initial test (Fig. 10.4) an $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2 - ThF_4}$ melt containing $100~\mathrm{ppm}$ of protactinium was treated with an excess of $\mathrm{UO}_2$ more than sufficient to precipitate all the protactinium. Surprisingly, only a small fraction $(\sim 10\%)$ of the protactinium was precipitated. A subsequent addition of hyperstoichiometric $\mathrm{UO}_2$ (an oxidant), however, caused a sharp increase in the amount of protactinium precipitated. Hydrogen sparging of the mixture returned much of this protactinium to solution, while subsequent addition of $\mathrm{NiF_2}$ (an oxidant) again increased the amount of protactinium precipitated. It is

![](images/a3d2366cec1c60a6522e951c81f42d6f4e8bce3ce836170365bc2128688438c1.jpg)  
Fig. 10.4. Effect of Oxidizing and Reducing Agents on the Precipitation of Protactinium Oxide from LiF-BeF $_2$ -ThF $_4$ -UF $_4$ (72-16-12-0.25 Mole %) at $625^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . The initial protactinium concentration was 100 ppm.

clear from these results that the extent of protactinium precipitation as oxide increases with increased oxidation of the system. It seems likely that a $\mathrm{Pa(V)}$ oxide, presumably a pure phase or a solid solution of $\mathrm{Pa}_2\mathrm{O}_5$ with $\mathrm{ThO}_2$ , was the principal oxide formed in these tests and in the previous tests of Shaffer et al. $^{13}$

In order to characterize more directly the protactinium oxide precipitated, an $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2 - ThF_4}$ mixture (72-16-12 mole $\%$ ) containing 3000 ppm protactinium was extensively hydrofluorinated with HF at $\sim 0.7$ atm to dissolve any oxides initially present and to oxidize the protactinium to the pentavalent state. This was followed by equilibration at several temperatures with small amounts of $\mathrm{ThO_2}$ added stepwise in a leak-tight stirred vessel $^{15}$ assembled in a glove box suited for work with alpha-active material. The results obtained are shown in Fig. 10.5 as the number of moles of protactinium in solution plotted vs total moles of $O^{2 - }$ added. It can be seen that at $563^{\circ}C,$ the experimental points agree well with a line calculated on the assumption that the protactinium was precipitated quantitatively as $\mathrm{Pa}_2\mathrm{O}_5$ . From this we conclude that the precipitated oxide is pure or nearly pure $\mathrm{Pa}_2\mathrm{O}_5$ .At higher temperatures the amount of protactinium in solution was well above the line corresponding to complete precipitation. Calculating the amount of oxide ion in solution by a material balance based on the

![](images/e14be9ac8c59f6dce4a1f6ee3c4837ed392905857158efb771fe782a0a5f1eb3.jpg)  
Fig. 10.5. Precipitation of Protactinium Oxide from LiF $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ -ThF $_4$ (72-16-12 Mole %) by addition of $\mathrm{ThO}_2$ . The line corresponds to the quantitative precipitation of $\mathrm{Pa}_2\mathrm{O}_5$ .

amount of $\mathrm{ThO_2}$ added and the amount of protactinium presumed to have precipitated as $\mathrm{Pa}_2\mathrm{O}_5$ , we estimated the solubility product of $\mathrm{Pa}_2\mathrm{O}_5$ as

$$
1 / 2 \mathrm {P a} _ {2} \mathrm {O} _ {5} (\mathrm {c}) \rightleftharpoons \mathrm {P a} ^ {5 +} + 5 / 2 \mathrm {O} ^ {2 -}, \tag {1}
$$

$$
\log \left(X _ {\mathrm {P a} ^ {5 +}} X _ {\mathrm {O} ^ {2 -}} ^ {2. 5}\right) = 4. 7 3 - 1 6. 5 \left(1 0 ^ {3} / T\right).
$$

In another experiment excess $\mathrm{ThO_2}$ and NiO were added to define, respectively, the oxide ion concentration and the redox potential. The resulting protactinium concentrations (Fig. 10.6) were used to calculate the equilibrium quotient for the reaction

$$
\begin{array}{l} 5 / 4 \mathrm {T h F} _ {4} (\mathrm {d}) + 1 / 2 \mathrm {P a} _ {2} \mathrm {O} _ {5} (\mathrm {c}) \\ \rightleftharpoons \mathrm {P a F} _ {5} (\mathrm {d}) + 5 / 4 \mathrm {T h O} _ {2} (\mathrm {c}), \tag {2} \\ \end{array}
$$

giving

$$
\log \left(X _ {\mathrm {P a} ^ {5 +}} / X _ {\mathrm {T h} ^ {4 +}} ^ {5 / 4}\right) = 5. 9 4 - 9. 9 8 \left(1 0 ^ {3} / T\right).
$$

![](images/4fae3426aaf3e4d5ee4511330cd94a9fc97ffc2f7fba56102b2de095f3d79cdf.jpg)  
Fig. 10.6. Solubility of Protactinium Oxide in LiF-BeF $_2$ . ThF $_4$ (72-16-12 Mole %) at Saturation in the Presence of an Oxidant (NiO).

Combining Eqs. (1) and (2) we obtain the following estimate for the solubility product of $\mathrm{ThO}_2$ in the MSBR solvent salt:

$$
\log \left(X _ {\mathrm {T h} ^ {4 +}} X _ {\mathrm {O} ^ {2 -}} ^ {2}\right) = - 0. 9 7 - 5. 2 2 \left(1 0 ^ {3} / T\right). \tag {3}
$$

Combining this with the previously measured equilibrium<sup>2</sup>

$$
\mathrm {T h O} _ {2} (\mathrm {s s}) + \mathrm {U F} _ {4} (\mathrm {d}) \rightleftharpoons \mathrm {T h F} _ {4} (\mathrm {d}) + \mathrm {U O} _ {2} (\mathrm {s s}), \tag {4}
$$

$$
\log Q = 2. 3 7 7 \left(1 0 ^ {3} / T\right),
$$

we estimate in turn the following solubility product of $\mathbf{U}\mathbf{O}_2$ :

$$
\log \left(X _ {\mathrm {U} ^ {4 +}} X _ {\mathrm {O} ^ {2 -}} ^ {2}\right) = - 0. 9 7 - 7. 6 0 \left(1 0 ^ {3} / T\right). \tag {5}
$$

These estimates of the solubility products of $\mathrm{ThO_2}$ [Eq. (3)] and $\mathrm{UO_2}$ [Eq. (5)] in LiF-BeF $_2$ -ThF $_4$ (72-16-12 mole %) are in reasonably good agreement with previous estimates<sup>16</sup> of these quantities in $\mathrm{Li_2BeF_4}$ melts, considering the uncertainties involved and the change in medium. We are thus encouraged to believe that the protactinium was precipitated in these tests as pure or nearly pure $\mathrm{Pa_2O_5}$ and that the present estimate of its low solubility is a usefully accurate one.

Upon combining reactions (2) and (4) we obtain

$$
\begin{array}{l} 5 / 4 \mathrm {U F} _ {4} (\mathrm {d}) + 1 / 2 \mathrm {P a} _ {2} \mathrm {O} _ {5} (\mathrm {c}) \\ \rightleftharpoons \mathrm {P a F} _ {5} (\mathrm {d}) + 5 / 4 \mathrm {U O} _ {2} (\mathrm {s s}), \tag {6} \\ \log \left[ \frac {X _ {\mathrm {P a F} _ {5}}}{X _ {\mathrm {U F} _ {4}} ^ {5 / 4}} a _ {\mathrm {U O} _ {2}} ^ {5 / 4} \right] = 6. 0 - 7. 0 (1 0 ^ {3} / T), \\ \end{array}
$$

which relates the saturation concentration of $\mathrm{PaF}_5$ in the presence of $\mathrm{Pa}_2\mathrm{O}_5(\mathrm{c})$ to the oxide ion concentration in the melt - here expressed in terms of the activity of $\mathrm{UO}_2$ - and the $\mathrm{UF_4}$ concentration. This, then, is a useful representation of the behavior to be expected in an MSBR fuel. In the absence of protactinium, sufficient oxide will cause the precipitation of a $\mathrm{UO}_2$ -rich ( $\sim 95$ mole $\%$ ) solid solution with $\mathrm{ThO}_2$ . Such a precipitate would establish a $\mathrm{UO}_2$ activity near unity (Fig. 10.7). With $\mathrm{PaF}_5$ present, equilibrium described in Eq. (6) would apply, showing clearly that the amount of $\mathrm{PaF}_5$ remaining in a solution saturated with $\mathrm{Pa}_2\mathrm{O}_5$ depends on the concentration of $\mathrm{UF_4}$ in the fuel and on the oxide activity. The behavior predicted from the present estimate of the equilibrium quotient for reaction (6) is indicated in Fig. 10.7. From this we conclude that it should be possible to selectively precipitate protactinium as pure or nearly pure $\mathrm{Pa}_2\mathrm{O}_5$ from an MSBR fuel salt without prior removal of uranium. This important conclusion will be verified by precipitation tests on melts containing both uranium and protactinium.

The redox potential required to produce the $\mathrm{PaF}_5$ in such a melt remained to be estimated. This was investigated by means of the reaction

$$
\mathrm {P a F} _ {5} (\mathrm {d}) + 1 / 2 \mathrm {H} _ {2} (\mathrm {g}) \rightarrow \mathrm {H F} (\mathrm {g}) + \mathrm {P a F} _ {4} (\mathrm {d}), \tag {7}
$$

![](images/f9548acbd2cc1da728799e9963fd20074c7ab2c4af06035ba989effcd238e222.jpg)  
Fig. 10.7. Concentration of $\mathsf{PaF}_5$ in LiF-BeF $_2$ -ThF $_4$ -UF $_4$ (72-16-12-0.3 Mole %) Saturated with $\mathsf{Pa}_2\mathsf{O}_5$ as a Function of the Activity of $\mathsf{UO}_2$ .

wherein $\mathbf{H}_2$ was sparged through the melt and the evolved HF was measured. The reaction apparently is too slow to provide equilibrium results by this transpiration technique, and, as a result, we can only estimate the following limits for the equilibrium quotient:

$$
Q = \frac {X _ {\mathrm {P a F} _ {4}} P _ {\mathrm {H F}}}{X _ {\mathrm {P a F} _ {5}} P _ {\mathrm {H} _ {2}} ^ {1 / 2}} \left\{ \begin{array}{l} \geqslant 0. 0 0 1 5 \text {a t} 5 5 0 ^ {\circ} \mathrm {C} \\ \geqslant 0. 0 1 \text {a t} 7 4 7 ^ {\circ} \mathrm {C}. \end{array} \right. \tag {8}
$$

Combining these results (8) with those of Long and Blankenship for the reduction of $\mathbf{U}\mathbf{F}_4$ to $\mathbf{U}\mathbf{F}_3$ by hydrogen, $^{17}$ we obtain

$$
Q _ {9} = \frac {X _ {\mathrm {P a} ^ {4 +}} X _ {\mathrm {U} ^ {4 +}}}{X _ {\mathrm {P a} ^ {5 +}} X _ {\mathrm {U} ^ {3 +}}} \left\{ \begin{array}{l} \geqslant 3 0, 0 0 0 \text {a t} 5 5 0 ^ {\circ} \mathrm {C} \\ \geqslant 1 2 0 0 \text {a t} 7 4 7 ^ {\circ} \mathrm {C}. \end{array} \right. \tag {9}
$$

The results of Eq. (9) suggest that it will be readily possible to maintain the protactinium sufficiently reduced in an MSBR by adjustment of the $\mathrm{UF_4 / UF_3}$

ratio so that insufficient $\mathbf{Pa}^{5+}$ is available to cause inadvertent precipitation as $\mathbf{Pa}_2\mathbf{O}_5$ .

The equilibrium quotients $Q_{6}$ and $Q_{9}$ may be used to estimate the conditions under which $^{233}\mathrm{Pa}$ might be isolated as $\mathrm{Pa}_{2}\mathrm{O}_{5}$ in an MSBR. If oxide is added to an isolation (decay) tank and the redox potential adjusted to precipitate $\mathrm{Pa}_{2}\mathrm{O}_{5}$ , then the total $^{233}\mathrm{Pa}$ content of the salt at equilibrium with the $\mathrm{Pa}_{2}\mathrm{O}_{5}$ ( $X_{\Sigma \mathrm{Pa}}$ ) should be given by

$$
X _ {\Sigma \mathrm {P a}} = Q _ {6} \left(\frac {X _ {\mathrm {U F} _ {4}}}{a _ {\mathrm {U O} _ {2}}}\right) ^ {5 / 4} \left[ 1 + Q _ {9} \frac {X _ {\mathrm {U F} _ {3}}}{X _ {\mathrm {U F} _ {4}}} \right]. \tag {10}
$$

From this expression it can be seen that the concentration of protactinium in the effluent from the decay tank will be minimized by (1) lowering $Q_{6}$ (i.e., lowering the temperature), (2) lowering the ratio of the $\mathrm{UF_4}$ concentration to the activity of dissolved $\mathrm{UO}_2$ $(a_{\mathrm{UO}_2})$ - presumably by increasing the latter, and (3) lowering the ratio of $\mathrm{UF_3}$ to $\mathrm{UF_4}$ . Based on the present estimates of $Q_{6}$ and $Q_{9}$ , it is thus predicted that with an $X_{\mathrm{UF_3}} / X_{\mathrm{UF_4}}$ ratio less than $10^{-6}$ and with an oxide concentration equivalent to a $\mathrm{UO}_2$ activity of 0.5, which should be insufficient to precipitate a uranium-rich oxide phase, it should be possible to lower the protactinium concentration of a fuel stream to $\sim 20$ ppm by precipitation of $\mathrm{Pa}_2\mathrm{O}_5$ .

The current program of investigation includes experiments which are designed to define the redox potential of the $\mathrm{Pa}^{5+}/\mathrm{Pa}^{4+}$ couple with high precision because of the significance of this value to the control of precipitation of protactinium as oxide in molten-salt reactors. Subsequently, the present estimate of the extent to which protactinium may be selectively precipitated as $\mathrm{Pa}_2\mathrm{O}_5$ in the presence of $\mathrm{UF}_4$ , without precipitation of $\mathrm{UO}_2$ as a pure phase or as an oxide solid solution, will be verified in direct tests.

# 10.6 ENTROPIES OF MOLTEN SALTS

# Stanley Cantor

The entropies of several classes of inorganic compounds in both the liquid and solid states are currently being correlated. An equation that has been developed to correlate entropy of binary compounds with molecular weight and density is:

$$
S _ {T} = A + \frac {n}{2} (5 R \ln M - 2 R \ln \rho_ {T}), \tag {1}
$$

where $S_{T}$ is the absolute molar entropy at temperature $T(^{\circ}\mathrm{K})$ , $R$ is the gas constant (1.98716 cal mole $^{-1}$ )

$\mathrm{^\circ K^{-1}}$ ）, $M$ is the gram-formula weight, $\rho_{T}$ is the density18 $(\mathrm{g / cm}^3)$ at temperature $T$ n is the number of atoms in the formula (e.g., $n = 2$ for LiF and for MgO, $n$ $= 3$ for $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ ), and $A$ is an empirical correlation constant that is dependent only on temperature and the formal charge of the constituent atoms. The constant $A$ is evaluated from measured entropies via Eq. (1). For example, using the experimental entropy of crystalline KCl at $298^{\circ}\mathrm{K}$ and Eq. (1) to evaluate $A$ , one obtains $A$ $= -20.38$ . When this value of $A$ is fed back into Eq. (1)

$^{18}$ Density data for almost all the compounds treated in the section were obtained from G. J. Janz et al., "Molten Salts: Vol. 1. Electrical Conductance, Density, and Viscosity Data," Natl. Std. Ref. Data Serv., Natl. Bur. Std. (U.S.), Washington 1968, No. 15.

to calculate $S_{298}$ of 24 other crystalline univalent (alkali, Ag, Cu, Tl) halides, the worst discrepancy between calculated and experimental $S_{298}$ is 1.6 cal mole $^{-1}$ K $^{-1}$ .

It can be shown that the form of Eq. (1) follows from Debye's theory of lattice heat capacity. [Indeed, Eq. (1) can be used to predict the maximum lattice vibrational frequency which is the scaling factor of Debye's theory.]

The entropies of molten univalent halides at $1000^{\circ}\mathrm{K}$ have now been correlated by Eq. (1). An average $A = 0.62$ was derived from 16 alkali halides for which experimental entropies are available. The results of the correlation are given in Table 10.1. Also in Table 10.1 are entropies of five other univalent halides. The

Data for CuCl through TlBr derived by combining ${S}_{298}^{ \circ  }\left( \mathrm{{cr}}\right)$ in NBS Technical Notes 270-3 and 270-4 with ${S}_{1000}^{ \circ  }\left( \mathrm{l}\right)  -$ ${S}_{298}^{ \circ  }\left( \mathrm{{cr}}\right)$ in K. K. Kelley, U.S. Bureau of Mines Bulletin 584. For RbBr and Rbl, ${S}_{298}^{ \circ  }\left( \mathrm{{cr}}\right)$ taken from K. K. Kelley and E. G. King, U.S. Bureau of Mines Bulletin 584, and combined with other calorimetric data from A. S. Dworkin and M. A. Bredig, J. Phys. Chem. 64, 269 (1960), and A. S. Dworkin, unpublished measurements. For CsF, ${S}_{298}^{ \circ  }\left( \mathrm{{cr}}\right)$ in I. E. Pawkov and F. S. Rachmenkalov, Russ. J. Phys. Chem. 43, 438 (1969), combined with A. S. Dworkin, unpublished measurements. Data for CsCl obtained by combining measurements in U.S. Bureau of Mines Reports of Investigations 6157 and 5832. For CsBr and Csl, ${S}_{298}^{ \circ  }\left( \mathrm{{cr}}\right)$ in M. Sorai, H. Suga, and S. Seki, Bull. Chem. Soc. Japan 41, 312 (1968), combined with calorimetric data of A. S. Dworkin and M. A. Bredig, op. cit., and A. S. Dworkin, unpublished measurements.

bCalculated from: $S_{1000}^{\circ}(\mathfrak{l}) = 0.62 + 5R\ln M - 2R\ln \rho_{1000}$

maximum discrepancy between calculated and experimental $S_{1000}$ for these cases is 1.4 cal mole $^{-1}$ deg $^{-1}$ .

When Eq. (1) is applied to the entropies at $1000^{\circ}\mathrm{K}$ of eight molten alkaline-earth halides (note that $n = 3$ ), the average value of $A = -13.4$ . The correlation is poorer than for the alkali halides; the worst discrepancy between calculated and experimental $S_{1000}^{\circ}$ is 6 cal mole $^{-1}$ deg $^{-1}$ .

The success in correlating the entropies of alkali and other univalent halides has led to extensions of Eq. (1) for estimating the entropies of more complex compounds. Among these are salts containing a complex anion (examples: fluoroborates, hydroxides, nitrates, carbonates, and sulfates). In these compounds it can be assumed that the anions exhibit "gaslike" degrees of freedom. In other words, in the melt the complex anions execute rotations and the atoms within the anion execute internal vibrations, both types of motion being unhindered by the immediate environment. When these assumptions are included in the calculated entropy, Eq. (1) is modified:

$$
S _ {T} = A + \frac {n}{2} (5 R \ln M - 2 R \ln \rho_ {T}) + S _ {\mathrm {a f r}} + S _ {\mathrm {a i v}}, \tag {2}
$$

where the subscripts afr and aiv refer to "anion free rotation" and "anion internal vibration." The last two entropy terms are calculated from standard equations of statistical thermodynamics:19

$$
S _ {\mathrm {a f r}} = R \ln (I T / \sigma) + 1 7 7. 6 6 7 (\text {l i n e a r a n i o n}), \tag {3a}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} S _ {\mathrm {a f r}} = \frac {R}{2} \ln I _ {x} I _ {y} I _ {z} + \frac {3}{2} R \ln T \\ - R \ln \sigma + 2 6 7. 6 4 0 (\text {n o n l i n e a r a n i o n}), \tag {3b} \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} S _ {\mathrm {a i v}} = R \sum_ {i = 1} ^ {3 m - 5 (\text {l i n e a r a n i o n})} \left[ (1. 4 3 8 7 \omega_ {i} / T) \right. \\ \times \left(e ^ {1. 4 3 8 7 \omega_ {i} / T} - 1\right) ^ {- 1} \\ \left. - \ln \left(1 - e ^ {- 1. 4 3 8 7 \omega_ {l} / T}\right) \right], \tag {4} \\ \end{array}
$$

where $I$ (with and without subscript) is moment of inertia $(\mathrm{g - cm}^2)$ , $\sigma$ is the symmetry number, $m$ is the number of atoms in the anion (e.g., 2 for $\mathrm{OH^{-}}$ , 5 for

$\mathrm{SO}_4^{2-})$ , and $\omega_{i}$ is the vibrational frequency20 $(\mathrm{cm}^{-1})$

In applying Eq. (2) to nine compounds with univalent anions, the constant $A$ at $1000^{\circ}\mathrm{K}$ was assumed to be the same (0.62) as for the univalent halides. The calculated and experimental entropies are given in the upper part of Table 10.2. Only LiOH exhibits a sizable discrepancy between observed and calculated values. For melts of divalent anions, the constant $A$ was obtained from Eq. (2) using the experimental $S_{1000}(l)$ of $\mathrm{Na}_2\mathrm{SO}_4$ (hence in Table 10.2 observed and calculated entropies for $\mathrm{Na}_2\mathrm{SO}_4$ must agree). Equation (2), with $A = -17$ , was then applied to four other alkali melts containing divalent anions. The results for these four, given in Table 10.2, show fairly good agreement, with $\mathrm{Li}_2\mathrm{CO}_3$ showing the worst discrepancy.

Table 10.2. Entropy at $1000^{\circ}\mathrm{K}$ of Molten Salts Containing Complex Anions   

<table><tr><td></td><td colspan="2">Entropy (cal mole-1K-1)</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Observeda</td><td>Calculated</td></tr><tr><td colspan="3">Univalent Anions</td></tr><tr><td>KHF2</td><td>61.7</td><td>60.5b</td></tr><tr><td>NaBF4</td><td>90c</td><td>89.1b</td></tr><tr><td>KBF4</td><td>91c</td><td>90.7b</td></tr><tr><td>LiOH</td><td>36.7</td><td>41.0b</td></tr><tr><td>NaOH</td><td>42.7</td><td>44.6b</td></tr><tr><td>KOH</td><td>47.6</td><td>48.0b</td></tr><tr><td>NaNO3</td><td>74.7</td><td>74.9b</td></tr><tr><td>KNO3</td><td>74.2</td><td>76.6b</td></tr><tr><td>AgNO3</td><td>75.9</td><td>78.7b</td></tr><tr><td colspan="3">Divalent Anions</td></tr><tr><td>Li2CO3</td><td>72.2</td><td>75.8d</td></tr><tr><td>Na2CO3</td><td>81.7</td><td>80.5d</td></tr><tr><td>K2CO3</td><td>84.9</td><td>84.7d</td></tr><tr><td>Na2SO4</td><td>93.8</td><td>93.8d (see text)</td></tr><tr><td>K2SO4</td><td>100.4</td><td>97.3d</td></tr></table>

Data taken directly from JANAF tables or derived by combining data in K. K. Kelley, U.S. Bureau of Mines Bulletin 584, and K. K. Kelley and E. G. King, U.S. Bureau of Mines Bulletin 592.   
bCalculated from: $S_{1000}^{o}(l) = 0.62 + 5R\ln M - 2R\ln \rho +$ $S_{\mathrm{afr}} + S_{\mathrm{aiv}}$   
cExperimentally obtained from reaction equilibria.   
$d_{\text{Calculated from:}}$ $S_{1000}^{6}(\mathbf{l}) = -17 + 7.5 \ln M - 3R \ln \rho + S_{\text{afr}} + S_{\text{aiv}}$ .

The experimental entropies of the two lithium compounds, LiOH and $\mathrm{Li_2CO_3}$ , are both considerably less than the calculated values. A possible explanation for this discrepancy is that the anionic rotation is not totally free - perhaps the relatively large electric field strength of the lithium ions acts to hinder the free rotation of the anion.

# 10.7 ALL-METAL CONDUCTANCE CELL FOR USE IN MOLTEN SALTS

G. D. Robbins J. Braunstein

Electrically insulating materials which are inert to molten fluorides are rare. Previously, we have employed conductance cells constructed of silica with molten $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2}$ mixtures,[21] over ranges of temperature and composition where the degree of attack was small and did not influence the conductance results. This imposed an undesirable limitation on the range of salt compositions and temperatures amenable to study. For example, it did not prove possible to supercool these melts in the presence of silica, probably due to the presence of corrosion product precipitates. Thus information on that part of the liquid range where transport properties are changing most rapidly with temperature – the supercooled region – could not be investigated. Drawing on the experience of previous investigators of molten fluorides,[22] a new conductance apparatus has been designed and constructed[23] in which only metal contacts the molten fluoride melts. The cell is designed to permit conductance measurements in an inert atmosphere and is compatible with the currently employed silica and Lavite furnace.[24] Provision is also made for the periodic addition of solid salt under a protective atmosphere.

Figure 10.8 is a schematic drawing of the metal conductance apparatus. The melt is contained in a nickel cup (6.6 cm ID, 9 cm high) resting on three silica pegs and enclosed in a silica well 8.3 cm in diameter and 34 cm high. The silica well fits into a silica and Lavite

$^{21}$ G. D. Robbins and J. Braunstein, "Electrical Conductivity Measurements in Molten Fluoride Mixtures, and Some General Considerations on Frequency Dispersion," pp. 443-47 in Molten Salts: Characterization and Analysis, ed. by G. Mamantov, Marcel Dekker, New York, 1969.   
22See, for example, the survey of experimental approaches in G. D. Robbins, J. Electrochem. Soc. 116, 813 (1969).   
23The advice and assistance of T. R. Mueller (Analytical Chemistry Division) and R. W. Poole and C. W. Wright, Jr., of the Fabrication Department, Oak Ridge National Laboratory, is gratefully acknowledged.   
24K. A. Romberger, MSR Program Semiann. Progr. Rept. Feb. 29, 1968, ORNL-4254, p. 149.

furnace²⁴ which permits visual observation of its interior.

ORNL-DWG70-9648

![](images/965f85c0150b1742852b1afd4a80ff889f5db2383464bc33b86bac92fa508e1a.jpg)  
Fig. 10.8. Schematic Representation of Conductance Apparatus.

The two platinum electrodes are concentric cylinders Heliarc welded to unplatinized platinum rods. The outer electrode is $4\mathrm{cm}$ in diameter and $1.5\mathrm{cm}$ high, while the dimensions of the inner cylinder are $0.5\times 0.5$ cm. The two replaceable electrodes are rendered immobile relative to each other by means of a Lavite spacer holding tightly fitted nickel rods into which the platinum support rods are inserted and held by nickel screws. This affords the capability of modifying the electrode size and configuration to accommodate varying electrochemical demands. The Lavite spacer also serves as a heat baffle to reduce convective currents in the glass envelope. The three electrode supports rise through the Pyrex cell top (joined to the silica well by a 102/75 ground glass joint) through heavy-walled Pyrex pipes. Double O-ring seals provide protection against atmospheric contamination. The maximum vertical displacement of the electrode assembly is $8\mathrm{cm}$ . The length of the silica well is such that the upper portion of the apparatus shown in Fig. 10.8, including the ground joint, is above the cooling collar and outside the furnace.

Two Teflon core stopcocks admit and release an inert cover gas. If gas bubbling into the melt is desired, the retractable nickel bubbling tube is used. The movable nickel thermocouple well and bubbler are admitted in the same manner as the electrode supports.

A Pyrex bulb for adding frozen salt to the melt is shown joined to the top by a ground glass joint. Normally a Pyrex cap fills this joint while salt is loaded into the addition chamber in an inert-atmosphere dry box. While rapidly expelling inert gas, the addition chamber is positioned onto the glass joint. Depression of the plunger lowers the Teflon cone, permitting salt to fall down the silica addition tube which terminates just above the nickel cup. In this way salt additions are made with minimum exposure to atmospheric moisture.

The cell has been satisfactorily leak tested (empty) up to $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . Room temperature tests in dilute aqueous KCl and $\mathrm{KCl - H_2O}$ -dioxane mixtures are currently in progress and indicate a cell constant of the order of 0.1 $\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ .

# 10.8 GLASS TRANSITION TEMPERATURES IN NaF-BeF $_2$ MIXTURES

G. D. Robbins J. Braunstein

In the recent development of transport theories of molten salts, $^{25-27}$ attention has been focused on the lower end of the liquid range - the supercooled region. Supported by the concept of a vanishing free volume $^{28}$

or configurational entropy $^{29,30}$ at temperatures above $0^{\circ}\mathrm{K}$ , it has been suggested $^{25,27}$ that the heat capacity of a supercooled liquid (in metastable equilibrium) must drop rapidly, as the temperature is reduced, to values near those of the crystalline phase. This is in order to avoid the implausibility of an (amorphous) liquid phase having lower entropy than the corresponding crystal. The temperature of such a transition has been termed a "theoretical glass transition temperature," $T_{0}$ , and is thought to lie near, but somewhat below, the experimental glass transition temperature, $T_{g}$ . Attempts at comparing $T_{0}$ obtained from analysis of conductance data from supercooled nitrate $^{25}$ and acetate $^{31}$ melts with experimental glass transitions have previously been reported. No such information is available for molten fluorides.

As part of an investigation of transport and thermodynamic properties in molten fluorides, glass transition temperatures have been measured by differential thermal analysis $^{32}$ for three glass-forming $\mathrm{NaF - BeF_2}$ mixtures. These melt compositions correspond to the congruently melting compound $\mathrm{NaBeF}_3$ (mp $376^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ), the eutectic at 55 mole $\% \mathrm{BeF}_2$ ( $365^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ), and 60 mole $\% \mathrm{BeF}_2$ (liquidus $= 405^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ). $^{33,34}$

DTA samples were prepared from optical-grade single-crystal chips of NaF obtained from Harshaw Chemical Company and vacuum-distilled $\mathrm{BeF}_2^{35}$ which had been previously treated with $\mathrm{H}_2 / \mathrm{HF}$ followed by

hydrogen. These were ground to 100 mesh in a controlled-atmosphere box (moisture content $< 0.1$ ppm, continuously monitored), and 10-g quantities of the requisite weighed proportions ( $\pm 0.1$ mole $\%$ ) were mixed on a rotating tumbler. Approximately 3 g of each mixed material was subsequently loaded into predried nickel DTA sample tubes, evacuated at $100^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ and welded closed. Heating for approximately 20 hr at $850^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ with intermittent inversions and shaking was followed by quenching into ice water. The exterior of the nickel cell (wall thickness $= 0.5\mathrm{mm}$ ) experienced the $850^{\circ}$ temperature drop in approximately 2 sec.

Figure 10.9 shows reproductions of actual pen traces from DTA warmups at heating rates of $2^{\circ} / \mathrm{min}$ for the three compositions. The ordinate represents the temperature difference (in microvolts) between one thermocouple in an aluminum oxide reference sample and a second thermocouple monitoring the fluoride glass, the temperature of which is shown as the abscissa. The upper portion of the figure depicts duplicate warmups on glass formed by quenching the 50 mole $\%$ $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ mixture. The initial part of the trace corresponds to the difference in heat absorption of the $\mathrm{Al_2O_3}$ and glass sample with its characteristic heat capacity, $C_{p,\mathrm{glass}}$ . At $117^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ the heat capacity of the sample exhibits a pronounced change corresponding to an endothermic process. This is interpreted as the glass transition, subsequent heating reflecting the heat capacity of a mobile amorphous phase persisting until the onset of crystallization.

Similar behavior is exhibited by the eutectic composition, as shown in the center section of Fig. 10.9. Here trace 1 corresponds to the glass formed by cooling $850^{\circ}$ in $\sim 2$ sec. After observation of the glass transition but prior to the onset of crystallization, the sample was slowly cooled $(100^{\circ}$ in $\sim 4$ hr), followed by a second warmup. The same value of $T_{g} = 117(\pm 2^{\circ}\mathrm{C})$ was observed as at the higher quenching rate. Traces 3 and 4 are a repeat of the experiment at a different heating rate after which the temperature was again cycled past $T_{g}$ following crystallization (trace 5). The absence of thermal effects in cycle 5 is consistent with the interpretation that the double peaks in the 200 to $250^{\circ}$ region represent crystallization of the liquid, the resulting crystalline phase exhibiting no solid state transitions over this interval and, of course, no glass transition.

The lower portion of the figure represents results obtained on a quench from the primary phase field of $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ , and again $T_{g}$ 's of 117 ( $\pm 2^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ) were observed. Examination of the glass of the eutectic composition by powder x-ray diffraction and polarizing light petrography $^{36}$ did not indicate the existence of microscopic phase separation. Thus it appears that over the composition range investigated here, the experimental glass transition temperature of 117 ( $\pm 2^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ) is invariant with composition.

$^{36}$ G. D. Brunton, private communication, Oak Ridge National Laboratory.

![](images/5e0fce0d01d6fbd581cfef5b6f3148610410101663597834d654755e6939136a.jpg)  
Fig. 10.9. Differential Thermal Analysis Traces of $\mathrm{NaF - BeF}_2$ Mixtures.

$$
\begin{array}{l}\text {ORNL - DWG 70 - 4817}\\50 \text {mole} \% \mathrm {BeF}_2 (\mathrm {M.P.} = 376^\circ)\\\text {QUENCHED} 850^\circ \rightarrow 0^\circ \mathrm {IN} \sim 2 \sec \quad \mathrm {H.R.} = 2\% \min\end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l}55\text{mole}\% \mathrm{BeF}_{2}(\mathrm{EUTECTIC} = 365^{\circ})\\ \\ 1\quad \text{QUENCHED} 850^{\circ}\mathrm{-}0^{\circ}\mathrm{IN}\sim 2\sec \quad \mathrm{H.R.} = 2\% \min \\ 2\quad \text{COOLED} 100^{\circ}\mathrm{IN}4\mathrm{hr}\quad \mathrm{H.R.} = 2\% \min \\ 3\quad \text{QUENCHED} 850^{\circ}\mathrm{-}0^{\circ}\mathrm{IN}\sim 2\sec \quad \mathrm{H.R.} = 2\% \min \\ 4\quad \text{COOLED} 100^{\circ}\mathrm{IN}4\mathrm{hr}\quad \mathrm{H.R.} = 1\% \min \\ 5\quad \mathrm{H.R.} = 1\% \min \end{array}
$$

$$
60 \text {mole} \% \mathrm {BeF}_{2} (\text {LIQUIDUS} = 405^{\circ}) \\ \text {QUENCHED} 850^{\circ} - 0^{\circ} \mathrm {IN} \sim 2 \sec \mathrm {H.R.} = 2\% \min
$$

# 10.9 CORRELATIONS OF ELECTRICAL CONDUCTIVITIES IN MOLTEN LiF-BeF $_2$ -ThF $_4$ MIXTURES

G. D. Robbins A. S. Gallanter<sup>37</sup>

In a previous report<sup>38</sup> we communicated specific electrical conductance data for six molten lithium-beryllium-thorium fluoride mixtures ranging in composition from 73-16-11 to 60.5-28.0-11.5 mole % LiF-BeF $_2$ -ThF $_4$ . Among these were several proposed MSBR fuel compositions. For these mixtures specific conductances were well represented as linear functions of temperature from near the liquidus to $650^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . In order to interpolate between the experimental compositions, and as an aid in estimating conductances of nearby compositions, attempts to obtain a smoothly varying correlation of conductance as a function of composition have been made.

A number of correlations were tested: (1) for the specific conductance (the conductance of one cubic centimeter of melt), (2) for the conductance of one equivalent of fluoride ions, $\Lambda_{\mathbf{F}}$ , and (3) for the conductance per equivalent of lithium ions, $\Lambda_{\mathrm{Li}}$ . These are related as $\Lambda = \kappa / C_{\mathrm{eq}}$ , where $C_{\mathrm{eq}}$ is the concentration (in equivalents per cubic centimeter) of the assumed charge-carrying constituent and $\rho$ is the density. Hence,

$$
\Lambda_ {\mathrm {F}} = \frac {\kappa}{\rho} \left(\frac {X M _ {\mathrm {L i F}} + Y M _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}} + Z M _ {\mathrm {T h F} _ {4}}}{X + 2 Y + 4 Z}\right)
$$

and

$$
\Lambda_ {\mathrm {L i}} = \frac {\kappa}{\rho} \left(\frac {X M _ {\mathrm {L i F}} + Y M _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}} + Z M _ {\mathrm {T h F} _ {4}}}{X}\right),
$$

where $X, Y,$ and $Z$ are mole fractions of LiF, $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ , and $\mathrm{ThF}_4$ , respectively, and $M$ with subscript is formula weight. Densities were calculated assuming additivity of molar volumes. $^{39}$

Isothermal values of $\kappa, \Lambda_{\mathbf{F}}$ , and $\Lambda_{\mathbf{Li}}$ were first plotted against mole fraction and equivalent fractions of each

individual component where equivalent fractions, $X^{\prime}$ $Y^{\prime}$ , and $Z^{\prime}$ , are defined as

$$
X ^ {\prime} = \frac {X}{X + 2 Y + 4 Z},
$$

$$
Y ^ {\prime} = \frac {2 Y}{X + 2 Y + 4 Z},
$$

and

$$
Z ^ {\prime} = \frac {4 Z}{X + 2 Y + 4 Z}.
$$

Fairly smooth correlations were obtained for $\kappa$ and $\Lambda_{\mathbf{F}}$ vs $X^{\prime}$ , as shown in Fig. 10.10. The curves shown represent the expressions:

$$
\Lambda_ {\mathrm {F}} = P _ {1} t \left(^ {\circ} \mathrm {C}\right) + P _ {2} X ^ {\prime} + P _ {3} X ^ {\prime 2} + P _ {4} t X ^ {\prime 2},
$$

$$
\kappa = P _ {5} t \left(^ {\circ} \mathrm {C}\right) + P _ {6} X ^ {\prime} + P _ {7} X ^ {\prime 2} + P _ {8} t X ^ {\prime 2}.
$$

![](images/4ab079bc8f34b327f762c6b7051811de498eab4c64b3077ea569d561c08d3a21.jpg)  
Fig. 10.10. Conductance of $\mathbf{LiF - BeF_2 - ThF_4}$ Mixtures vs Equivalent Fraction LiF.

Table 10.3. Parameter Values for Correlation Functions   

<table><tr><td>Equation:</td><td>ΛF = P1t(°C) + P2X&#x27; + P3X&#x27;2 + P4tX&#x27;2</td></tr><tr><td>Parameter values:</td><td>P1 = 5.860 × 10-2, P2 = -123.04, P3 = 122.38, P4 = 0.15841</td></tr><tr><td>σ:</td><td>0.316</td></tr><tr><td>Equation:</td><td>κ = P5t(°C) + P6X&#x27; + P7X&#x27;2 + P8tX&#x27;2</td></tr><tr><td>Parameter values:</td><td>P5 = 4.728 × 10-3, P6 = -9.686, P7 = 10.56, P8 = 1.0625 × 10-2</td></tr><tr><td>σ:</td><td>0.024</td></tr><tr><td>Equation:</td><td>ΛF = P9 + P10t(°C) + P11t(X - Z)</td></tr><tr><td>Parameter values:</td><td>P8 = -30.17, P10 = 3.435 × 10-2, P11 = 9.774 × 10-2</td></tr><tr><td>σ:</td><td>0.366</td></tr><tr><td>Equation:</td><td>κ = P12 + P13t(°C) + P14t(X - Z)</td></tr><tr><td>Parameter values:</td><td>P12 = -2.193, P13 = 2.589 × 10-3, P14 = 7.458 × 10-3</td></tr><tr><td>σ:</td><td>0.027</td></tr></table>

$P^{\prime}$ s with subscripts are parameter values and are listed in the upper portion of Table 10.3 together with the standard error of fit, $\sigma$ . These expressions in $X^{\prime}$ and $t$ were obtained by least-squares computer fitting of $Y$ $(\Lambda_{\mathbf{F}}$ or $\kappa$ ) vs $X^{\prime}$ at constant temperature, from which it appeared that

$$
Y = a + b X ^ {\prime} + c X ^ {\prime 2}
$$

adequately represented the isothermal data. Furthermore, $a$ , $b$ , and $c$ appeared to be linear functions of temperature. Expressing them as such and substituting into the equation for $Y$ resulted in a six-parameter representation of $Y = f(X', t)$ . Eliminating various combinations of parameters and comparing the results both graphically and with $o$ , the representations given in the upper portion of Table 10.3 were determined.

One linear combination of mole fractions, namely $X - Z$ , resulted in a useful linear correlation of $\kappa$ and $\Lambda_{\mathbf{F}}$ . These plots are shown in Fig. 10.11, where the lines represent the least-squares equations listed in the lower portion of Table 10.3. Equations of this form were determined in the manner described for $Y = f(X', t)$ , except that the isothermal representations were found to be linear functions of the argument. No doubt other more complex combinations of composition would also produce smooth correlations.

It is not to be expected that extrapolations of these correlation functions to distant regions of the phase diagram should accurately predict values of conductance. The primary utility of the correlations lies in the

ternary composition region near which the data are reported.

![](images/01b192a012b7043d8d9a2456268dcf68e581a61e75814b610e99f5168bd183b2.jpg)  
Fig. 10.11. Conductance of $\mathsf{LiF - BeF}_2\cdot \mathsf{ThF}_4$ Mixtures vs Difference in Mole Fractions of LiF and $\mathsf{ThF_4}$

# 10.10 COORDINATION EFFECTS ON U(IV) SPECTRA IN FLUORIDE MELTS

L.M.Toth

The investigation of coordination effects on U(IV) spectra in molten fluorides has continued in an effort to broaden our understanding of how these effects influence both the spectrum and the reactivity of pertinent transition metal ions in solution. Spectra demonstrating a change from one species present in fluoride-rich low-temperature melts to another present in fluoride-deficient high-temperature melts have been shown previously.[40] The characteristic transitions of each species are listed in Table 10.4 along with absorption coefficients of some major peaks.

Previous experience with transition metal ions in molten salts indicates that these changes originate primarily from an interaction of the solvent anions in the first coordination sphere with central metal ion. Identification of these changes could, in principle, be accomplished by energy-level calculations. However, this method is, at the moment, not refined to the point where changes as shown in Table 10.4 for the actinides can be unequivocally identified. A more direct method is by comparison of the melt spectra with crystal spectra where the coordination of the cation has been described by an independent means - x-ray crystal

logy. Such an approach is ordinarily hampered by a lack of corresponding single-crystal structural information. However, fluoride single crystals have been under examination for some years now at this laboratory, $^{42}$ so that an accumulation of fluoride crystal structural information is available.

Host crystals of general formula $\mathrm{A}_x\mathrm{Th}_y\mathrm{F}_{4y + x}$ (where A is an alkali metal) doped with approximately 1 mole $\%$ U(IV) in the sites of known fluoride ion coordination number have been grown. Spectra of these U(IV) crystals have been measured from 25 to $550^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . Representative spectra of 9, 8, and 7 coordination are shown in Figs. 10.12-10.14, with characteristic peaks and noteworthy absorption coefficients listed in Table 10.4. Comparison of the two melt species spectra (Table 10.4) with 9-, 8-, and 7-coordinated U(IV) crystal spectra at $550^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ (Table 10.5) leads to the conclusion that the fluoride-rich species is $\mathrm{UF_8}^{4 - }$ and the fluoride-deficient is $\mathrm{UF_7}^{3 - }$ - apparently in an equilibrium which is dependent upon the $\mathbf{F}^{-}$ concentration:

$$
\mathrm {U F} _ {8} ^ {4 -} \rightleftharpoons \mathrm {U F} _ {7} ^ {3 -} + \mathrm {F} ^ {-}.
$$

The identification of the $\mathrm{UF_8}^{4-}$ species in solution is based on the close agreement of the melt spectrum with

Table 10.5. Characteristic Transitions for 9-, 8-, and 7- Coordinated U(IV) in Fluoride Host Crystals $\mathbf{K}\mathbf{T}\mathbf{h}_{2}\mathbf{F}_{9}$ $\mathbf{K}_7\mathbf{Th}_6\mathbf{F}_{31}$ ,and $\mathrm{Cs_3ThF_7}$ ,respectively,at $550^{\circ}C$ Taken from Figs. 10.12-10.14 of this report Absorption coefficients in liters mole ${}^{-1}\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ for some major peaks are given in parentheses
